REPORTS OF Libraries Sub-Committees. 192 REPORT OF THE CLAPHAM PUBLIC LIBRARY SUB-COMMITTEE, For the Year ended 31st March, 1911. The Sub-Committee have pleasure in presenting the TwentyFourth Annual Report on the work of the Clapham Library. The number of books issued during the year was 148,062 in the Lending Library and 22,718 in the Reference Library, being a total issue of 170,780 volumes, and a daily average of 727 volumes for the 235 days on which the library was open. These figures are the highest of any hitherto recorded, and show an increase in issues of 11,194 over the previous year, and a daily average increase of 62 volumes. The number of books now in stock is 19,122, of which 15,554 are in the Lending Library and 3,568 in the Reference Library. 4,419 readers' ordinary tickets (including renewals) and 1,359 students' tickets were issued during the year in the Lending Library. In consequence of all the available shelf room in the Reference Library being filled with books, three additional bookcases have been erected in that department, under the superintendence of the Borough Engineer and Surveyor. The Sub-Committee desire to express their cordial thanks to the donors of various books, periodicals, pictures, etc. The Borough Council in November, 1910, re-appointed the Mayor (Alderman Dawnay, J.P.), Alderman Lidiard, and Councillors Bulcraig, J.P., Cox, Cundall, Duncan, Henderson and 193 Young as the Sub-Committee for the Clapham Library for 1911 Councillor Cundall was re-elected chairman, with a vote of thanks for his services in the chair during the previous year. The Sub-Committee are again pleased to record their satisfaction with the efficient manner in which the Librarian and his Staff have discharged their duties. On behalf of the Sub-Committee, JAS. W. CUNDALL, Chairman. 1st May, 1911. 194 REPORT OF THE PUTNEY LIBRARY SUB-COMMITTEE, For the Year ended 31st March, 1911. The Sub-Committee have pleasure in presenting the TwentyThird Annual Report of the Library. In December, 1910, Councillors Gosden, Hoile, Jackson, Mathias and Radford were appointed by the Borough Council to act as Sub-Committee for the year 1911. Councillor Gosden was elected Chairman for the year. During the year a large sum of money was spent on the purchase of new books in order to make the new edition of the catalogue, to be issued early in the year 1911-12, as complete as possible. The Sub-Committee anticipate that the issue of the new catalogue will give an impetus to the issues of books from the lending department. The annual estimates for the year amounted to £1,279, the estimated product of a penny rate in the Putney Ward, plus a balance from the previous year of £121. The Sub-Committee desire to thank the donors of various books and newspapers to the library. After considering the hours of opening the library the SubCommittee resolved that on and after January 1, 1911 the reading rooms should remain open until 10 p.m. In order to give shop assistants the oportunity of using the lending department it was decided to keep that department open on Thursdays. ending Department. The stock in the Lending Department has increased by 1,724 volumes; of these 1,686 were acquired by purchase, and 38 were presented. One hundred obsolete volumes were withdrawn from stock, and 279 worn out books were replaced, making a total of 12,915 volumes now available for the use of borrowers. L+ 195 The issues have increased by 1,554 volumes, the total number being 75,643 as compared with 74,089 issued during the year 1909-10. On March 31st 2,225 borrowers' tickets and 300 students' tickets were available for use in the department. The sum of £55 1s 0d. was received from the sale of catalogues, fines, etc., during the year. Reference Department. The Reference Library is shelved in open cases, a plan greatly appreciated by readers. No record is kept of the number of books consulted, but it is estimated that upwards of 150 persons use the room daily. During the year 19 volumes have been added to the stock, making the total 2,42 volumes. Magazine Room. Thirty-five monthly magazines and reviews, six quarterlies and 52 weekly periodicals are placed in these rooms, which continue to be used by an increasing number of readers. The magazines and reviews are transferred to the Lending Department when replaced by new issues and are greatly in demand for home reading. News Room. The list of newspapers, etc., provided in this room includes 31 daily newspapers, among which are Birmingham, Cardiff, Dublin, Edinburgh, Liverpool, Manchester and Plymouth daily journals, 55 weekly newspapers and periodicals, and 62 monthly and quarterly publications. The only change in the staff was occasioned by the resignation of H. Rock, caretaker, whose position was filled by J. Roberts. A hearty vote of thanks was accorded to Councillor Mathias for his services as Chairman of the Sub-Committee during the year. The Sub-Committee again have much pleasure in recording their satisfaction with work of the Librarian and his Staff. Signed on behalf of the Putney Library Sub-Committee, ALFRED W. GOSDEN, Chairman. 196 REPORT OF THE STREATHAM, BALHAM, AND TOOTING LIBRARIES SUB-COMMITTEE, For the Year ended 31st March, 1911. In submitting the Twentieth Annual Report on the work of the Streatham, Balham, and Tooting Libraries the Sub-Committee have pleasure in again recording a considerable increase in the use made of the three libraries during the past year as indicated by the large number of books issued. The circulation at each library was as follows:— Institution. Lending Library. For Reference purposes. Total. Average Daily Issue. The Tate Library 190,154 11,103 201,257 821 Balham Library 166,483 4,166 170,649 697 Tooting Library 112,234 2,188 114,422 467 Total Number of Volumes issued 486,328 1,985 The total number of books circulated represents an increase of 31,321 on last year's return. A detailed statement of the number issued in each class of literature is given in the general table of statistics, page 15. It is a matter of much satisfaction to the Sub-Committee to find such convincing evidence of continued public appreciation of the usefulness and popularity of the libraries under their supervision. During the past twenty years the number of books issued 197 has increased from 120,744 to 486,328, and the growth of the stock from 6,017 volumes, with which the Tate Library opened, to 43,240 the present aggregate stock of the three libraries. The Sub-Committee are also gratified to observe how extensively the libraries are used by students and others for acquiring knowledge and information on the many and varied subjects of study occupying their attention. It may be interesting to mention as indicating the educative value of the libraries that last year there were issued for home-reading no fewer than 48,179 books in the classes of literature comprising history, biography, travel, science, art, ethics, poetry, sociology, etc. The issue of 11,103 volumes for consultative purposes at the Tate Library shows that in the populous district of Streatham a suitable Reference Reading Room is greatly needed. The number of Borrowers' Tickets issued at each library during the year was as follows:— Institution. No. of Tickets issued. Ordinary Tickets. Students' Tickets. Total. The Tate Library, Streatham 5,491 2,324 7,815 Balham Public Library 4,083 578 4,661 Tooting Public Library 2,824 1,144 3,968 Total 12,398 4,046 16,444 All tickets are renewable every year. The students' tickets, are not available for works of fiction or books in the Juvenile Department. The stock at the 31st March, after making the necessary deductions on account of replacements and discarded books, numbered 43,240 volumes, located as follows:— 198 Volumes in Stock. The Tate Library, Streatham 20,558 Balham Public Library 13,255 Tooting Public Library 9,427 Total 43,240 The classification of the stock is given in the table on page 16. The limited amount available for the maintenance of the three libraries makes it difficult for the Sub-Committee to provide copies of popular books in sufficient number to meet the increasing demand and also precludes the purchase at present of many important works of reference. Owing to the large circulation the sum required for the replenishment of worn-out stock is becoming every year a heavier charge on the book funds. At the annual stocktaking in July the number of books which could not be accounted for was four. The following is a statement of the receipts from fines, sale of catalogues, etc., at the three libraries:- £ s. d. The Tate Library, Streatham 143 15 0 Balham Public Library 94 9 2 Tooting Public Library 61 6 5 Total £299 10 7 At each library the work of cataloguing has occupied a good deal of attention. Revised editions of four catalogues of Fiction and Juvenile Literature (in the form of Indicator-Keys) and two Lists of Recent Additions were published at intervals during the year. The rapid growth of the Tooting Library also made it necessary to compile and print earlier than was anticipated a new General Subject-Catalogue of the books in the Lending Department. 199 During the ensuing year the Sub-Committee hope to proceed with the re-cataloguing of the books in the Tate Library. This work has now become a matter of urgency owing to the numerous and important additions that have been made to the collection in recent years and to the fact that several supplementary lists are now out of print. In the meantime, borrowers are reminded that some of these minor difficulties might be greatly mitigated if more frequent and careful use were made of the card catalogues, which, when used in conjunction with the printed catalogues, form a complete index to the contents of each library. The Reading Rooms continue to be used by a very large number of the inhabitants of the district, and are open every weekday from 9 a.m. to 10 p.m., and on Sundays (except during the months of July, August and September) from 3 to 9 p.m. The Rooms are supplied with a comprehensive selection of the principal newspapers and periodicals, a list of which is appended to this Report, pages 17 to 26. In response to their application the Sub-Committee have received from H.M. Stationery Office a grant of 105 volumes of historical publications. By the liberality of Sir William J. Lancaster, J.P., the staircase at the Tooting Public Library has been provided with white Pentelicon marble treads for the better preservation of the stone steps. In July, the Reading Rooms and Lending Department of the Tate Library were re-painted and decorated, and advantage was taken of the closing of the building to have part of the furniture re-polished. Signed on behalf of the Sub-Committee, THOS. W. WATSON, Chairman. 29th May, 1911. 200 REPORT OF THE WANDSWORTH LIBRARIES SUB-COMMITTEE. For the Year ended 31st March, 1911. The Libraries Sub-Committee for the Parish of Wandsworth beg to report on the work of the Wandsworth Public Libraries for the 26th year of their existence. The Members of the Council which form the Sub-Committee are: Councillors Harrison (Chairman), The Mayor, Councillors Couzens, Everett, B.A., Evershed, Junr., J. A. Robinson, and Shillington. The Issues of books have increased from 162,300 to 180,587or an increase of over 11 per cent. The Libraries are now clear of debt, the last instalment of loans having been paid off during the year. Electric lamps with metallic filaments have been substituted at the Branches in Allfarthing Lane and Garratt Lane. The Libraries were closed on the day of His Late Majesty's Funeral, 20th May, 1910. The new edition of the Encyclopaedia Britannica has been purchased and has proved of much service. The springs of the doors at Allfarthing Lane Branch Library have been repaired. 201 On page 15 will be seen a table of issues from each Library. During the year 3,064 borrowers' tickets and 650 students' tickets have been issued. The Sub-Committee are grateful for gifts of books, periodicals, etc. The sum of £102 6s. Id. had been paid into the bank, being receipts from fines, sale of catalogues, etc. There have been no changes in the staff during the year. A hearty vote of thanks was given to Councillor Couzens for his services in the chair during the past year. The Sub-Committee again desire to express their appreciation of the able and efficient way in which the Librarian and his Assistants have discharged their duties during the past year, also of the helpful assistance given by the Librarian to inquiring students and visitors generally. Signed on behalf of the Wandsworth Libraries Sub-Committee. W. J. HARRISON, Chairman. 22nd September, 1911. 202 CLASSIFICATION AND NUMBER OF BOOKS ISSUED. Clapham Library. The Newnes Library, Putney. PAGE Violence, Mortality from 142 Vital Statistics of Sub-Districts 28 Vital Statistics of Whole Borough 26 Wandsworth, House-to-house Inspections in 169, 170, 171 Wandsworth : Table of Causes of Death 232, 233 Water Supply, Cutting-off Notices 150 Whooping Cough 95 Workshops, Summary of Principal Trades carried on 204, 211 Zymotic Diseases, Deaths from 49 Zymotic Diseases, Deaths in External and Internal Institutions 50, 51 Zymotic Diseases, Death-rate from 50, 52 Zymotic Diseases, Death-rate per 1,000 for Five Years and 1910 115 Zymotic Diseases, Death-rate per 1,000, 1900-1910 52 REPORT Of the Medical Officer of Health for the Year 1910. To the Mayor, Aldermen and Councillors of the Metropolitan Borough of Wandsworth. Sir and Gentlemen, I have the honour to present the Annual Report on the health and sanitary condition of the Borough for the year 1910. Population. The population of the Borough as estimated by the Registrar General was, in the middle of 1910, 305,838, an increase of 8,192 compared with 1909, but this estimate, in my opinion, is much under the actual population. The population on which all the rates in this Report are calculated has been obtained in a similar manner to that adopted since 1905, by dividing the number of inhabitants at the Cerisus, 1901, by the number of rated hereditaments in that year. The factor thus obtained represents the number of persons per rated hereditament, by which the number of hereditaments in the middle of 1910 is multiplied. 2 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. It is probable, however, that this calculation gives a population somewhat in excess of the actual population, as the number of persons per rated hereditament may have decreased. As the current year (1911) is a Census year the population will be fully corrected before the middle of the year. In the meantime, however, the method adopted, in my opinion, gives the closest approximation to the actual population. The population for the purposes of the Equalisation of Rates Act was estimated in 1906 as 296,020, in 1907, 308,663, in 1908, 318,603, in 1909, 327,322, and in 1910, 332,843, these populations being calculated from the number of rated houses. Compared with 1909, the greatest increase in population occurred in Streatham, next in Wandsworth, and the smallest in Putney. The corrected population in Table II. is used to calculate the urates in this Report. The difference between the corrected and uncorrected population is made up by the population in the Public Institutions of the Borough, which are as follows:— Clapham:—Hostel of God. Putney:—The Priory, Roehampton. Streatham:—St. Anne's Home. (St. Pancras parish.) Middlesex County Lunatic Asylum (part of). Jews' Home, Nightingale Lane. 4 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Tooting:—Tooting- Home. Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospital — The Fountain and the Grove. Newlands Asylum. Tooting Bee Asylum. Wandsworth:—The Workhouse. Royal Hospital for Incurables. Middlesex County Lunatic Asylum (part of). H.M. Prison. The total population of these Institutions was about 6,300, but as a proportion of the inmates of the Tooting Home, the Workhouse, and the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals belong to this Borough, a deduction of 5,900 made from the uncorrected total population gives, approximately, the corrected population, viz.:—343,800, compared with 339,100 in 1909,, 329,700 in 1908, and 320,120 in 1907. Births. During the 52 weeks ending the 31st December, 1910, 6,967 births, 3,570 of males and 3,397 of females, were registered, compared with 6,985 in 1909, 7,331 in 1908, 7,036 in 1907, and 7,057 in 1906. The total number of births registered in this Borough was. 6,873, but 166 births occurred in Institutions outside the Borough. of persons belonging to the Borough, and 72 births in Institutions in the Borough of persons not belonging to the Borough, making a corrected total of 6,967. Of the 166 births which were registered outside the Borough, all occurred in Lying-in Institutions, 93 in Clapham Maternity, 43 in the General Lying-in Hospital, and 30 in various Institutions in other parts of London. The birth.rates for the year in all the sub.districts and in the Borough as a whole show a considerable reduction compared with the decennial average, while compared with the previous year there has been a slight reduction in all the sub.districts with the exception of Streatham, where there is a slight increase in this rate. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 7 The birth-rate for the whole of London was 23.85 per 1,000, compared with 24.3 in 1909, and 27.22 the decennial average; while for England and Wales this rate was 24.8 per 1,000, compared with 25.6 in 1909, and 27.47 the decennial average. With regard to this rate, although, as the Registrar General states in his Report for 1908, the crude birth-rate, i.e., the ratio of births to population at all ages, is valuable for comparing the rates from year to year, or for ascertaining the rate of natural increase, yet it is not of great value for any inquiry into the significance of the variations which occur over a period of years, on account of the changing constitution of the population, or for comparing the rate in different communities, on account of the differences in age and sex distribution. It is necessary therefore to calculate the proportion of births to the number of possible mothers, or the total number of females living in the Borough at ages 15 to 45 years. In the year 1901 this rate was 88.6 per 1,000, in 1907, 74, in 1908, 73, in 1909, 69.4, and in 1910, 68.2; while for England and Wales the rates were, in 1901, 114.2, and in 1909, 102.3. The legitimate birth-rate, calculatcd on the married female population was in 1901, 215 per 1,000, in 1908, 174, in 1909, 165, and in 1910, 164; while for England and Wales it was, in 1901, 234.2, and in 1909, 209.4. The low rates in this Borough are due to the greater proportion of females, both married and unmarried, at ages 15.45, compared with the whole of England and Wales, and also to the lower birth-rate. Although these rates are, owing to the lapse of time since the last Census, only approximately correct, yet there is no doubt that a very serious reduction has taken place in the birth-rate during the last 10 years. 170 births occurred in the Workhouse during the year and of these 10 belonged to Clapham, eight to Putney, 18 to Streatham, 11 to Tooting, 51 to Wandsworth, 64 to Battersea, and eight to other places. 8 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Of these 170 births 106 were illegitimate, seven belonging to Clapham, four to Putney, 13 to Streatham, 10 to Tooting, 25 to Wandsworth, 41 to Battersea, and eight to other places. In the Borough, excluding the Workhouse, 128 illegitimate births occurred, 24 in Clapham, 11 in Putney, 42 in Streatham, 16 in Tooting, and 35 in Wandsworth. The total number of illegitimate births was 185, 31 of which belonged to Clapham, 15 to Putney, 55 to Streatham, 26 to Tooting, and 58 to Wandsworth, compared with 175 in 1909, and 177 in 1908. The percentage of illegitimate births to total births was 2.65, compared with 2.50 in 1909, 2.41 in 1908, 2.68 in 1907, and 3.5 for the whole of London for 1909. In October, 1909, the Local Government Board made an order declaring the above Act in force in the Borough from and after the 30th November, 1909. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 9 This has entailed a considerable amount of clerical work, as m addition to keeping a Register of the births notified, a copy has to be sent to the London County Council each week. So far as can be ascertained at present little advantage has resulted from the Act, as a very large proportion of the births are not notified, and many of these are cases which would benefit by a visit from the Council's Inspector. I am of opinion that if registration of the births were made compulsory within seven days much greater good would result, and the present duplicate system could then be abolished. During the 52 weeks ending the 31st December, 1910, 2,856. deaths, 1,401 of males and 1,455 females were registered, compared with 3,279 in 1909, 3,230 in 1908, and 3,337 in 1907. Compared with 1909 there has been a decrease of 423 deaths,, and of 1,058 compared with the decennial average corrected for increase of population. The uncorrected death-rate for the Borough was 8.16 per 1,000, compared with 9.50 in 1909, 9.63 in 1908, and 10.24 in 1907. The average uncorrected rate for the decennium 1900-1909 was 11.28, or 3.12 above the rate for the year 1910. 11 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In the sub-districts these rates were as follows—7.31 in Clapham, 7.44 in Putney, 6.48 in Streatham, 18.79 in Tooting, and 7.35 in Wandsworth. The reason for the high rate in Tooting is that in that subdistrict there are a number of Public Institutions, as the Tooting Bee Asylum and the Tooting Home, where persons from other parts of London are received and among whom the mortality is very great. To ascertain the corrected death-rate corrections must be made to the above by the addition of the deaths in Out-lying Institutions of persons belonging to the Borough, and the subtraction of all deaths in Public Institutions in the Borough of persons not belonging thereto, whose domiciles were in other parts of London. The total number of deaths for the 52 weeks, corrected for both internal and external Institutions was 3,105, compared with 3,520 in 1909, 3,380 in 1908, 3,416 in 1907, 3,461 in 1906, and 4,055 the decennial average corrected for increase of population. During the year 618 deaths occurred in Public Institutions and 105 in other places not institutions in the Borough. Of the total of 723 deaths, 564 were of persons not belonging and 159 of persons belonging to the Borough. Table VIII. shows the deaths in Public Institutions in the Borough of persons not belonging thereto, arranged as to ages, causes of death, and Institution in which the death occurred. The greatest number of deaths was due to Old Age, and of the 138 registered, 111 occurred in Tooting Bee Asylum, 13 in the Tooting Home, six in the Workhouse, and four in the Middlesex County Asylum. In the Middlesex County Asylum the greatest number of deaths occurred from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, Old Age, disease of the Respiratory Organs, Bright's disease, Enteritis, and all other causes, which include all diseases of the nervous, system. In Tooting Bec Asylum the greatest number of deaths occurred from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, Old Age, Softening of the Brain, Cancer, and all other causes; in the Hostel of God from Tubercular disease of the Lungs and Cancer; and in the Tooting Home from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, Old Age, and all other causes. In the Fever Hospitals 10 deaths occurred from Scarlet Fever, compared with 18 in 1909, 15 from Diphtheria, compared with 28, two from Lobar Pneumonia, one from Measles, one from, Tubercular Meningitis, and one from Accident. 14 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In the next Table the number of deaths in Institutions outside the Borough is given in detail, but a complete list of these Institutions with the number of deaths that occurred in each during the year, is given after Table I. of the Local Government Board's Tables. As the Borough is dealt with as one Sanitary District the deaths of persons belonging to other parishes in the Borough in Institutions such as the Fever Hospitals, Tooting, are considered to be deaths in Internal Institutions with regard to the whole Borough, and are not included in the following Tables. The same applies to deaths in the Hostel of God, Clapham, the Tooting Home, the Workhouse, the Tooting Bee Asylum, and the Royal Hospitals for Incurables. 18 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The total number of deaths in out-lying Institutions was 812, 469 of males and 343 of females, compared with 897 in 1909, 783 in 1908,739 in 1907, and 731 in 1906. 395 of these deaths occurred in Workhouses and Workhouse Infirmaries, compared with 436 in 1909; 407 in General and Special Hospitals, 90 of which occurred in Public Lunatic or Imbecile Asylums, compared with 447 in 1909; and 10 in the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals, compared with 14 in 3909. Of the 407 deaths in General and Special Hospitals, 67 occurred in St. Thomas's Hospital, 29 in Bolingbroke Hospital, 10 in Belgrave Hospital, 21 in Victoria Hospital, 24 in St. George's Hospital, 13 in Westminster Hospital, 12 in King's 'College Hospital, 11 in Guy's Hospital, 13 in Long Grove Asylum, 13 in Horton Asylum, 13 in Banstead Asylum, 15 in Charing Cross Hospital, 10 in Cane Hill Asylum, five in Middlesex Hospital, and nine in West London Hospital. In the South Western Hospital of the Metropolitan Asylums Board, there were five deaths, one from Scarlet Fever, one from Diphtheria, one from Enteric Fever, and two from other causes. In the Western Hospital three from Enteric Fever, and in Gore Farm Hospital two from Scarlet Fever. The number of deaths in these and other Institutions outside the Borough is shown on Table XV. The corrected number of deaths after adding the deaths of persons belonging to the Borough and subtracting those of persons dying in the Borough and not belonging thereto, was 3,105, 1,555 males and 1,550 of females, a decrease of 415 compared with 1909, and of 950 compared with the corrected decennial average. The corrected death-rate for the year was 9-03, compared with 10.38 in 1909, 10.25 in 1908, 1067 in 1907, 11.15 in 1906, and 12.24 the rate for the 10 years 1900-1909. 20 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The death-rate in England and Wales in 1910 was 13.4 per 1,000, compared with 14.5 in 1909, 14.7 in 1908, 15.0 in 1907, 15.4 in 1906, 16.2 in 1905, and 16.2 in 1904; in the 77 Great Towns 14.3, compared with 14.7 in 1909, and 14.9 in 1908; and in the 136 Smaller Towns 12.9, compared with 13.9 and 14.0. If a comparison is to be made with other places it is necessary to again correct the corrected rate, viz.:—9.03, for age andi sex distribution. This is done by multiplying the rate by a factor obtained bydividing the standard death-rate in England and Wales, at theCensus of 1901, with the standard death-rate for the Borough at the same period. This factor has been ascertained to be 1.0547, which gives a rate for this Borough for the year 1910 of 9.52 per 1,000, compared with 10.94 in 1909, 10.81 in 1908, 11.25 in 1907, 11.76 in 1906, 12.24 in 1905) 13.60 in 1904, 12.87 in 1903, and 14.00 in 1902. In the separate sub-districts of the Borough the corrected death-rate was 8.15 in Streatham, 8.93 in Putney, 8.99 in Clapham, 9.61 in Wandsworth, 10.37 in Tooting. The rates in all the sub-districts were much lower than in 1909, and were by far the lowest ever recorded. The rates in this Table do not agree with the rates in Table XIV., as the rates in the later Table are calculated on the total population without deducting the population of non-parishioners in Public Institutions in the Borough. The first Chart shows the death-rate in the Borough for each fortnight, compared with the quinquennial average 1905-1909. The Chart shows that only on two fortnights was the rate above that for the previous quinquennium, viz.:— on the fortnights ending the 3rd and 17th December. During the rest of the year the fortnightly rate was under, and specially in March and September much under, the quinquennial rates. The highest rate, 13.07, occurred during the fortnight ending the 17th December, and this was due to the great number of deaths from Respiratory diseases owing to climatic conditions. The next Chart shows the Annual Death-rate per 1,000 during each week of the year for the whole of London and for each fortnight for the Borough of Wandsworth. During the whole year the rate in this Borough was under the rate for the whole of London. The highest rate in London was in the first week of December, and the lowest in the first week of August. The four Tables which follow are inserted by order of the Local Government Board. Table II. shows the population, the number of births and deaths registered, and the number of deaths under one year of age, for the whole Borough, as well as for the various subdistricts. It also shows these for each year since 1900, and is to a large extent a complete history of the mortality of the Borough for the last 10 years. Note. —The deaths to be included in Column 7 of this table are the whole of those registered during the year as having actually occurred within the district or division. The deaths to be included in Column 12 are the number in Column 7, corrected by the subtraction of the number in Column 10 and the addition of the number in Column 11. By the term "Non-residents" is meant persons brought into the district on account of sickness or infirmity, and dying in public institutions there; and by the term "Residents" is meant persons who have been taken out of the district on account of sickness or infirmity and have died in public institutions elsewhere. The "Public institutions" to be taken into account for the purposes of these tables are those into which persons are habitually received on account of sickness or infirmity, such as hospitals, workhouses and lunatic asylums. Under 1 year. 1 to 5 years. 5 to 15 years. 15 to 25 years. 25 to 65 years. 65 years and upwards Clapham 13.44 7.84 4.00 3.52 37.28 33.92 Putney 14.10 8.10 3.87 3.87 36.26 33.80 Streatham 13.93 6.85 3.26 4.71 36.30 34.95 Tooting 26.90 8.48 4.68 3.21 33.63 23.10 Wandsworth 21.27 7.78 3.83 4.77 34.34 28.01 Whole Borough 17.55 7.63 3.80 4.25 35.58 31.18 Compared with 1909 the percentage of deaths under one year was .48 higher; from one to five years 2.11 lower, due to the decrease in the number of deaths from Measles and diseases of the Respiratory Organs; from five to 15 years .01 lower; from 15 to 25 years .36 higher; from 25 to 65 years. 1.03 higher; and from 65 years and upwards '24 higher. There was a considerable diversity in these rates in the different sub-districts. In Streatham the rate under one year was only 13.93, while in Tooting it was 26.90, while over 65 years of age Tooting had a rate of 23.10 and Streatham of 34.95. Again, as was noted also in 1909, at from one to five years the rate was still high in Tooting and low in Streatham, and also at ages five to 15, while at ages 15 to 25, 25 to 65, and over 65 the Tooting rates were much lower and were the lowest of the five sub-districts. 32 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. These differences are due to the different age and sex consttution of the separate sub-districts, as well as to the differences insocial conditions. Infantile Mortality. The total number of deaths of infants under one year was545> compared with 601 in 1909, 720 in 1908, and 715 in 1907, and is 402 under the decennial average corrected for increase of population. Of these, 84 occurred in Clapham, 40 in Putney, 124 in Streatham, 92 in Tooting, and 205 in Wandsworth. The infantile mortality, or deaths under one year to 1,000 births was 78, compared with 86 in 1909, 98 in 1908, 101 in 1907,. 125 in 1906, 118 in 1905, 123 in 1904, and no in 1903. This rate was 67 in Clapham, compared with 83 in 1909 and 112 the decennial average; 81 in Putney, compared with 86 and' 118; 64 in Streatham, compared with 73 and 101 ; 92 in Tooting,, compared with 98 and 145; and 89 in Wandsworth, compared; with 89 and 119. In the whole Borough the rate was eight per 1,000 births under that for 1909, and 38 under the decennial average. The rate in 1910 was much lower than any rate ever recorded, and in each sub-district it shows a marked diminution as compared with the decennial average, in Tooting showing a difference of 53, in Clapham of 45, in Streatham and Putney of 37, and in Wandsworth of 30 per 1,000, compared with that average. The Tables which follow are inserted at the request of the Local Government Board and show in detail the deaths of infants under one year, arranged in monthly age periods and from certain diseases. Table XXII. shows the deaths in the whole Borough and the six following Tables the deaths in each registration sub-district. The total number of births was 6,967, of which 6,782 were legitimate and 185 illegitimate, and the total deaths 545. From the notifiable infectious diseases there was only one death, from Erysipelas, during the year. From Measles there were seven deaths, from Whooping Cough 30, from Diarrhœal diseases 76, from Wasting diseases 243, from Tuberculous diseases 14, from diseases of the Respiratory Organs 108, from Convulsions 19, from simple Meningitis six, from Suffocation, overlying, three, from Syphilis nine, and from all other causes 29. Compared with 1909 there has been a decrease in the number of deaths in all the groups with the exception of Syphilis. In all the deaths with one exception, in Streatham, the cause of death was certified by a medical man, or by a coroner after inquest. 40.9 per cent, of the deaths occurred in the first month, compared with 37.4 in 1909, 32.9 in 1908, and 33.7 in 1907, the increase being almost wholly due to the number of deaths from Wasting diseases; 59.4 per cent. in the first three months, compared with 54.4 in 1909, 55.1 in 1908, and 53 in 1907; and 74.5 per cent. in the first six months, compared with 73, 77.3 and 73. The Table also shows that 243, or 44.6 per cent. of the deaths occurred from Wasting diseases, and of these 141, or 58 per cent. cent. occurred under one month, a decrease of 15.5 per cent. compared with 1909. Of the total deaths under one month, viz.:—223, 177 or 80 per cent. occurred from Wasting diseases, compared with 8o.4 per cent. in 1909, 77 per cent. in 1908, and 74 per cent. in i907. 35 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. From Diarrhœal diseases only four deaths occurred under one month. The greatest mortality from these diseases occurred at one to two months, two to three months, and six to seven months. From Tuberculous diseases 14 deaths occurred, nine from Tuberculous Meningitis, one from Tuberculous Peritonitis, and four from other Tuberculous diseases, compared with 17 in 1909, and 26 in 1908; and 108 from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, compared with 115 and 134. Six deaths were registered as due to Meningitis, but enquiries of the medical attendant showed that all these were due to simple Meningitis. 36 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. (Table 5.) Table XXIX. shows approximately the proportion of deaths per 1,000 births for each week under one month, and for each month up to 12 months from five groups of diseases. Compared with 1909 the rate from common infectiousdiseases is lower, due to the increase in the number of deaths from Measles and Whooping Cough. There has again been a very large reduction in the rate from Diarrhceal diseases, the rate having been only 10.90, compared with 13,15 in 1909, and 20.72 in 1908. From all other causes there has also been a slight decrease compared with 1909. Compared with the rate for England and Wales for 1909, the rate for the common infectious diseases was 6.97 per 1,000 in England and Wales and 5.30 in Wandsworth, from Diarrhœal diseases 12.64 in England and Wales and 10.90 in Wandsworth, from Wasting diseases 41.69 in England and Wales and 34.87 in Wandsworth, from Tuberculous diseases 4.00 in England and Wales and 2.00 in Wandsworth, and from other causes 43.43 in England and Wales and 25.11 in Wandsworth. The total rate per 1,000 births was 108 in England and Wales and in Wandsworth 86 for 1909, while the rate in Wandsworth for 1910 was only 78, by far the lowest rate ever recorded. Compared with the rate in England and Wales for the five years 1904-1908 the rate in this Borough was in 1910, from the common infectious diseases 2.85 lower, from Diarrhceal diseases 12.0 lower, from Wasting diseases 8.59 lower, from Tuberculous diseases 3.08 lower, from other causes 24.16 lower, and from all causes 50.87 per 1,000 births lower. In the Table it will also be seen that the rate from the common infectious diseases was highest at 10 to 11 months, from Diarrhoeal diseases at two to three months, from Wasting diseases under one month, from Tuberculous diseases at five to six and 11 to 12 months, and from other causes under one month. 44 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. From all causes the rate was, as in the previous year, highest under one week and lowest at 11 to 12 months. Table XXX. shows the infantile mortality per 1,000 births from all causes and from certain causes for the whole Borough and for each sub-district. From Premature Birth the rate was lowest in Putney and highest in Tooting, from Development diseases highest in Wandsworth and lowest in Clapham, from Diarrhoeal diseases highest in Tooting and lowest in Putney, from Measles and Whooping Cough highest in Wandsworth and lowest in Clapham, from Respiratory diseases highest in Tooting and lowest in Streatham, from Tubercular diseases highest in Putney and lowest in Clapham, and from other causes highest in Tooting and lowest in Clapham. The Table also shows the rates for certain groups of diseases compared with the five previous years. In all the groups there has been a reduction compared with the average, but it is greatest in the case of the Diarrhœa rate. In the sub-districts the greatest reduction in this rate has occurred in Tooting and the least in Streatham. It was noted in the Report for 1909 that the Infantile mortality rate was the lowest on record, but in 1910 there was a still further reduction, viz. :—from 86 to 78 per 1,000, and this reduction occurred in all the sub-districts. The climatic conditions were this year again unfavourable for Diarrhoea and digestive diseases of infants, while the deaths from diseases of the Respiratory Organs were also influenced by these conditions. In July, August and September, the temperature was above the average on only 15 days out of 92, and then only slightly above, while the number of days on which rain fell was in July and August much above the average. 46 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In September, although the rainfall was small, yet the temperature remained low, and as was the case in 1909, during these three months dust and flies were conspicuous by their absence. The work of the Inspectors, both male and female, has also largely contributed to the reduction of this mortality, the former in securing more cleanly and sanitary conditions of houses and yards, and the latter in the education of mothers as to the proper methods of infant feeding. The higher infantile mortality rates in Tooting and Wandsworth were not due to directly preventible causes as will also be seen from a perusal of the Table. The causes of these higher rates are Premature Birth with Developmental diseases, and Respiratory diseases, causes which are not so amenable to control by the Sanitary Authority. In Tooting the rate from Premature Birth was 21, from Developmental diseases 20, and from Respiratory diseases 23 per 1,000 births; in Wandsworth 14.33, 21.71 and 21.27; whereas in Putney the rate from Premature Birth was 10.18, from Developmental diseases 18.32; and in Streatham the rate from Respiratory diseases was 7.29 per 1,000 births. 47 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The rate from the principal Zymotic diseases, viz.:—Smallpox, Measles, Scarlet Fever, Whooping Cough, Diphtheria, Enteric Fever, and Diarrhoea, was .54 per 1,000 (.47 in Clapham, .53 in Putney, .40 in Streatham, .72 in Tooting, and .68 in Wandsworth), compared with .81 in 1909, .82 in 1908, .97 in 1907, 1.36 in 1906, 1.30 in 1905, and 1.60 in 1904. In out-lying Institutions 58 deaths occurred from Zymotic diseases (eight belonging to Clapham, three to Putney, 14 to Streatham, four to Tooting, and 29 to Wandsworth), compared with 96 in 1909, 54 in 1908, 48 in 1907, 52 in 1906, 67 in 1905, and 58 in 1904. Of these deaths 34 occurred in Workhouse Infirmaries, 13 from Measles, one from Influenza, 16 from Whooping Cough, two from Diarrhoea, and two from other Septic diseases; 13 in General and Special Hospitals, one from Whooping Cough, three from Diarrhoea, three from other Continued Fevers, one from Puerperal Fever, one from Erysipelas, and four from other Septic diseases; three in Public Lunatic Asylums, two from Measles and one from Diarrhoea; and eight in the Hospitals of the Metropolitan Asylums Board, three from Scarlet Fever, one from Diphtheria, and four from Enteric Fever. 50 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In Institutions in the Borough 29 deaths from Zymotic diseases occurred of persons not belonging thereto, compared with 57 in 1909, 82 in 1908, 106 in 1907, and 90 in 1906. The decrease in 1910 is due to the decrease in the number of deaths from Scarlet Fever and Diphtheria. Of these deaths 26 occurred in the hospitals of the Metropolitan Asylums Board at Tooting, 10 from Scarlet Fever, and 15 from Diphtheria. One death from Measles occurred in the Grove Hospital, one death from Influenza in the Middlesex County Asylum, and one from Enteric Fever in H.M. Prison. In Institutions in the Borough 22 deaths from Zymotic diseases occurred of persons belonging thereto, 21 in the Fever Hospitals at Tooting, nine from Scarlet Fever, and 12 from Diphtheria. Compared with 1909, this shows a decrease of three deaths from Scarlet Fever, and of seven deaths from Diphtheria. Scarlet Fever. 687 cases of Scarlet Fever were notified during the year, compared with 987 in 1909, 1,232 in 1908, and 1,330 in 1907. 106 of these were in Clapham, 51 in Putney, 199 in Streatham, 100 in Tooting, and 231 in Wandsworth. 579 or 84 per cent, of these cases were removed to the hospital where 12 died, and 108 or 16 per cent, were treated at home with no death. The total number of deaths was 12, of which three were in Clapham, none in Putney, four in Streatham, two in Tooting, and three in Wandsworth. The percentage of cases removed to hospital, viz. : 84, is higher than in 1909, when it was 82 per cent. In 1908, 88.8 per cent. were removed; in 1907, 84.8; in 1906, 83.8; in 1905, 84.1; in 1904, 81.6; in 1903, 78.1; in 1902, 75; and in 1901, 70.6 per cent. The case mortality was 1.77 per cent. compared with 14.2 in 1909, 1.7 in 1908, 1.65 in 1907, 1.78 in 1906, 1.87 in 1905, and 1.74 in 1904. The mortality among cases removed to hospital was 1.6 per cent. compared with 1.6 in 1909, and among those treated at home no death occurred, compared with .57 in 1909. The mortality among the home-treated cases was thus much lower than among those cases treated in hospital. 61 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The proportion of cases notified per 1,000 of population was 2.0, compared with 2.88 in 1909, 3.67 in 1908, 4.08 in 1907, 3.4 in 1906, 5.1 in 1905, and 2.8 in 1904, and in the County of London 2.15, compared with 3.56, 4.60, 5.53, 4.3, 4.1, and 2.89. The proportion of cases notified was thus .15 per 1,000 lower in this Borough than for the whole of London, while in 1909 it was .68 per 1,000 lower. Scarlet Fever and Schools. The next Table shows the number of cases of Scarlet Fever for each month of the year among children in attendance at elementary and other schools for each sub-district, also the number for the whole Borough, the number of cases among persons not at school, the number attending schools outside the Borough, as well as the number of cases in which, as far as could be ascertained, the diagnosis of Scarlet Fever was incorrect. Of the total number of cases, viz. :—687, the diagnosis was incorrect in 55 or 8 per cent.; 350 or 51 per cent, were in attendance at schools in the Borough; 21 or 3 per cent, attended schools outside the Borough ; while 261 or 37 per cent, did nol attend school, being either above or under school age. 64 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The percentage of children attending school was as follows : in Clapham, 57 out of 106, or 53 per cent, were at school; in Putney, 16 out of 51, or 31 per cent.; in Streatham, 113 out of 199, or 56 per cent.; in Tooting, 38 out of 81, or 47 per cent.; and in Wandsworth, 126 out of 231, or 54 per cent. The percentage in Tooting is calculated after deduction' of the cases notified from Institutions in that sub-district. In Clapham the schools chiefly affected were Cavendish Road, in which 11 cases occurred, and Haselrigge Road, with nine cases. Five of the cases in Cavendish Road occurred in June. In Putney only 16 cases occurred among children attending schools in that sub-district, but several of the cases in Putney attended schools in the Wandsworth sub-district. In Streatham the schools chiefly affected were Oldridge Road with 18 cases, and Welham Road, 11 cases. In Tooting, Sellincourt Road was chiefly affected with 16 cases, six of these occurring in June. In Wandsworth this disease was slightly more prevalent than in the other sub-districts, and the schools chiefly affected were Eltringham Street with 24 cases, Brandlehow Road with 19 cases, the Wandle with 14 cases, and Southfields with 10 cases. In Eltringham Street six cases occurred in November and five in December, and in Brandlehow Road seven of the cases occurred in October. In both schools there were missed cases which were the cause of infection, but only in Brandlehow Road was it necessary to take any action, one of the classrooms having to be closed so that thorough disinfection could be carried out. 65 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In Clapham, out of a total of 49 cases, 21 or 45 per cent. occurred in children attending school; in Putney, of 16 cases, four or 25 per cent.; in Streatham, of 85 cases, 39 or 45.8 per cent.; in Tooting, of 63 cases, 30 or 47.6 per cent.; in Wandsworth, of 71 cases, 29 or 40.8 per cent.; and in the whole Borough, of 284 cases, 123 were in attendance at schools in the Borough, and 13 at schools outside the Borough, a total of 136, or 48 per cent. Of the remaining cases, viz. :—148, in 39 or 137 per cent., the diagnosis was incorrect, and 109 or 38 per cent. were not in. attendance at school. In Clapham the number of cases of Diphtheria in attendance at school was small. In six of the schools only one case occurred in each during the year, in four two cases, in one three cases, and in one four cases. In Putney also, only one case occurred in two schools, and two cases in one school. In eight months of the year in Putney no cases were notified among children attending school- In Streatham, Welham Road with nine cases and Eardley Road with seven were the highest. Five of the cases in the former school occurred in March, and five in the latter school in. September. The five cases in Eardley Road School all occurred in one classroom, and prompt measures were at once taken to prevent further extension of the disease, with the result that no further cases occurred in the school during the rest of the year. Of the five cases in Welham Road School in September, three were members of the same family. The sub-district of Tooting was also very free from Diphtheria for six months of the year, from April to September inclusive, no cases occurred. Ensham Street and Franciscan Road Schools had nine cases each, but in neither school was any particular classroom involved. In Wandsworth there were only 29 cases and these occurred at irregular intervals during the year. In six of the schools only one case occurred in each, in three two cases, in two three cases in each, in one four cases, and seven cases occurred in the various private schools. 75 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Enteric Fever. 54 cases of Enteric Fever were notified during the year (six from Clapham, four from Putney, 13 from Streatham, five from Tooting, and 26 from Wandsworth), compared with 66 in 1909, 60 in 1908, 70 in 1907, 96 in 1906, and 96 in 1905. 37 of the cases were removed to hospital where four died, and 17 were treated at home with four deaths. The total number of deaths was eight (one in Clapham, one in Putney, two in Streatham, none in Tooting, and four in Wandsworth), compared with six in 1909, seven in 1908, 10 in 1907, 15 in 1906, 11 in 1905, and 11 in 1904. The number of deaths was two more than in 1909, but 8.9 under the decennial average. 80 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The proportion of cases notified per 1,000 of population was 15, compared with .19 in 1909, .18 in 1908, .21 in 1907, .30 in 1906, and "33 in 1905, and with "26 for the whole of London. The corrected death-rate from Fever was .02 per 1,000, compared with .017 in 1909, .02 in 1908, .03 in 1907, '048 in 1906, .04 in 1905, and .04 in 1904, while the rate for the whole of London was .04, compared with .03 in 1909, .04 in 1908, .04 in 1907, .05 in 1906, .05 in 1905, and .06 in 1904. Of the 37 cases removed to hospital, 11 or 29 per cent. were found not to be suffering from Enteric Fever, compared with 32 per cent. in 1909. The Chart shows the number of cases notified during each week of the year. In 18 of the weeks during the year no cases were notified, one case each in 19 of the weeks, two cases each in 12 of the weeks, three cases each in two of the weeks, and four cases in one of the weeks. The maximum number of cases, viz.:—Four, was notified in the first week of November. Of the 54 cases notified 11 were not Enteric Fever, leaving 43 cases in which special enquiries were made as to the probable cause of the disease. In 14 of the cases the disease was probably contracted by eating shell-fish, in six it was contracted outside the Borough (one in Algiers, one in Margate, one in Boulogne, one in New York, and two in other places), in nine the infection was contracted from other cases, in four probably from watercress, four occurred in the Prison, while in six no history of infection could be ascertained. With regard to the 14 cases caused by shell-fish, five were from oysters, five from mussels, and four from winkles. In the cases contracted outside the Borough, six in number, it was impossible to ascertain the exact source of infection, but in three of the cases it was probably from infected water supply. 81 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Of the nine cases contracted from other cases, one was infected by a "carrier" case, and another in a neighbouring Borough was also infected by the same "carrier." The history of the "carrier" case was quite definite, and examination of the stools and urine bacteriologicallv showed that in the former numerous typhoid bacilli were present. The second, third and fourth examinations of the stools and urine, however, gave negative results. The "carrier" case contracted Enteric Fever in 1906 and was ill in all for about ten weeks. On the 26th January, of this year, he had an indefinite illness with severe abdominal pain and fever, and recovered in about 14 days. On the 26th February his nurse, who resided in a neighbouring Borough, was found to be suffering from Enteric Fever, and was removed to the Fever Hospital on the 9th March, where she died on the 15th March. The son, who resided in this Borough, was notified on the 16th March and removed to hospital, from which he was discharged on the 3rd June. A second case infected by a previous case was that of a medical student who had been in attendance on several cases in hospital. A third case was that of a girl in an Institution infected by a previous case which was not resident in the Institution, but resided in a neighbouring Borough. The six remaining cases infected by other cases had an interesting history, and the following special report was made on the matter, on the 10th November, to the Public Health Committee:— "10th November, 1910. "Enteric Fever in Wandsworth. "I have to report the occurrence of several cases of Enteric Fever in Wandsworth, due chiefly to a mild case which was not at first diagnosed as Enteric Fever. G 82 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "The particulars of the case are as follows:—On the19th September, a case of Enteric Fever was notified to me in the person of R. B., aged10 years, residing in Burtop Road, the first symptoms having occurred about the 7th September. At the time careful inquiries were made, but the source of infection could not be ascertained. After his return from hospital on the 8th November it was ascertained that this boy had had winkles, bought from a street vendor, on the 21 st August, and also about the same time had eaten what he called watercress which he had taken from the River Wandle,, near McMurray's Mills. "No further cases were notified until the 27th October,, when I received a certificate to the effect that J. J., aged four years, residing in Burtop Road, opposite the first case, was suffering from the disease, the first symptoms having occurred about the 16th October. It was not at first suspected that there had been any connection between this case and the first one. On the 3rd November, however, three cases, sisters of J. J., aged respectively six, eight, and nine years, were notified, the first symptoms occurring in each of those three about the 23rd September, and I then visited Burtop Road and ascertained that the only other child in the house, S. J., aged one-and-three-quarters years, had had a suspicious illness attended by sickness of diarrhoea, about the 1st October, and also that this infant had been sent to another house in Burtop Road to a nurse who was attending R. B., the first case, previous to his removal to hospital, and before the nature of the illness was suspected. At the same time I was informed that a girl, F. C., aged 22 years, who had been nursing the second case, J. J., was ill and feverish, and with the consent of the medical attendant I visited and found her to be suffering from a typical attack of Enteric Fever. "My attention was then directed to the boy of one-andthree-quarters years, and as I suspected that he had teen infected by R. B., and thus in turn infected the five other cases, and also as I had been informed that the nurse had taken the child to the house in Burtop Road where the first case occurred, I had a specimen of his blood taken for examination. The result of this examination was positive, a VVidal re-action having been obtained. To make the 83 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. nosis more certain, samples of the faeces and urine have been taken, and these are now under examination at the Lister Institute. I am, however, of opinion that this infant is convalescent from Enteric Fever, and that the first symptoms occurred about the end of September, a fortnight after it had been exposed to infection. R. B. was ill for nine days before his removal to hospital, and was being nursed by the same person who had charge of the infant without any precautions being taken. The nurse states that she prepared food both for the boy R. B. and for the boy J. J., and it is easy to understand how the infection could have been communicated. "The necessary disinfection has been carried out by the staff. "The infant is being treated at home under the supervision of the medical attendant, and I do not expect that any further cases will occur." The stools and urine of the infant S. J. were examined on two separate occasions, but on both a negative result was obtained. A Widal re-action was, however, obtained on a second examination. One of these cases, the nurse, aged 22 years, died in hospital. Another case occurred in the same street a few weeks after, and it was at first thought that this last case was also infected by S. J., but further inquiries showed that the patient had partaken of shell-fish purchased from the same vendor that supplied R. B. This last case was not notified for seven weeks after the first symptoms appeared, and died the same day that the notification was received. Of the total number of cases, viz.: 14, in which infection was probably caused by eating shell-fish, six died, and other six cases, one of which died, were infected by one of the cases caused by shell-fish. g 2 84 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Although a considerable amount of supervision is now carried out, and a large amount of useful work done by the Fishmongers' Company in preventing polluted shell-fish from being sold, yet without more stringent legislation on the matter it will be impossible to prevent similar outbreaks in the future. In only six of the cases which proved to be Enteric Fever •could no history of infection be ascertained, and one of these proved fatal. Of the ii cases which proved to be not Enteric Fever four proved fatal, one from Otitis, one from Empyema, one from Pneumonia, and one from Peritonitis. Cases of Mistaken Diagnosis. Erysipelas. 182 cases of Erysipelas were notified during the year and four deaths occurred. 32 of the cases and one of the deaths occurred in Clapham, eight cases and one death in Putney, 40 cases and one death in Streatham, 31 cases and no death in Tooting, and 71 cases and one death in Wandsworth. In 1909 the number of cases notified was 192, and the deaths eight. 35 of the cases were removed to the Union Infirmary, but none of these cases were fatal. Measles. During the year 44 deaths, 28 of males and 16 of females, were due to Measles, compared with 108 in 1909, 45 in 1908, 66 in 1907, 65 in 1906, 89 in 1905, and 104 in 1904. 12 of the deaths occurred in Clapham, five in Putney, eight in Streatham, four in Tooting, and 15 in Wandsworth. Seven of the deaths were of infants under one year, 34 from; one to five years, and three from five to 15 years; 93.2 per centof the deaths having occurred under five years of age. In all, 4,617 cases were notified, compared with 4,342 in 1909. 3.753 in 1908, and 4,079 in 1907. 90 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 904 of these were cases of Measles, 1,010 of Chicken-pox, 919 of Whooping Cough, 1,274 of Mumps, and 510 of Ringworm. There was an increase in the number of cases of Chicken-pox, Whooping Cough and Mumps, and a decrease in the number of cases of Measles and Ringworm. With regard to Measles, 253 were notified from schools in Clapham, 225 in Putney, 202 in Streatham, 130 in Tooting, and 94 in Wandsworth. The schools chiefly affected were : in Clapham, Haselrigge Road and St. Andrew's Street; in Putney, Hotham Road, St. Mary's and All Saints; in Streatham, Oldridge Road, Broadwater Road and Fircroft Road; in Tooting, Tooting Graveney; and in Wandsworth, Earlsfield Schools. There was no widespread epidemic of this disease either in 1909 or 1910, but in the current year it is already, at the beginning of the year, epidemic, and the number of cases and deaths will be much greater compared with the two previous years. The following pamphlet is now left at every house where a case of Measles has been notified from school, and also in all other known cases:— "PRECAUTIONS AGAINST MEASLES. "1.—Measles is a dangerous infectious disease and is very fatal to young children. In the five years 1905-1909 the total number of deaths from Measles in London was 9,260, or more than four times greater than the number of deaths from Scarlet Fever. "2.—It is very highly infectious, especially in the early stage before the rash appears. "The first symptoms of the disease are coughing, sneezing, and redness of the eyes. "3.—A child suffering from Measles must not be sent to school until four weeks after the appearance of the rash. 91 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "4.—A child suffering from Measles should, if possible, be placed in a separate room, with a good fire burning in it, and the window should be opened so as to admit some fresh air without allowing the room to become cold. The bed should be placed in the part of the room where there is least draught. The fire should be lighted even in summer so as to get some ventilation. "Where a separate bedroom cannot be got, the above instructions should still be carried out. "5.—As the death-rate from Measles is high, parents are advised in every case to call in a doctor at once. "6.—All the other children in the house who have not had Measles before, must be kept away from school, but the other children living in the house who have had Measles may continue at school. "The same applies to the attendance of children at any public meeting such as Church, Sunday-school, or Children's Treat. "It is important to remember that children rarely take true Measles more than once in their lives. "7.—When a case of Measles has occurred in a house, the other children should be carefully watched, so that on the first appearance of illness they may be kept at home and properly treated. "8.—When a child in any house is suffering from Measles, no neighbour's or friend's children must be admitted to the house. The child who is suffering from Measles must not be allowed to play with other children for one month after the appearance of the rash. "9.—Disinfection. When a child has recovered, notice should be at once sent to the Medical Officer of Health, who will have proper disinfection carried out. After disinfection it is necessary for the clothing of the patient, as well as the bed linen and blankets, to be washed and aired. The room 92 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. should be scrubbed out and all washable articles of furniture should be washed. The windows of the room should be left open for one or two days, and as much sunlight as possible admitted. "The skin of the child who has been ill should be thoroughly cleansed by several washings with soap and warm water. "P. Caldwell Smith, M.A., M.D., D.P.H., Camb., "Medical Officer of Health. Whooping Cough. 68 deaths were registered during the year, 37 of males and 31 of females, an increase of one compared with 1909, but a decrease of 13 compared with the decennial average. Nine of the deaths occurred in Clapham, eight in Putney, 16 in Streatham, nine in Tooting, and 26 in Wandsworth. 30 of the deaths were of infants under one year, 37 of children from one to five years, and one from five to 15 years. The death-rate was .20 per 1,000, compared with .19 in 1909, and '27 for the whole of London in 1910. This disease was slightly more fatal in London and in this Borough in the year 1910 than in the two previous years. In the sub-districts of Clapham, Streatham and Wandsworth it was. slightly more prevalent than in the other two sub-districts. The number of cases notified from schools was 919, compared with 538 in 1909, 612 in 1908, 255 of these being from schools in the sub-district of Wandsworth, the schools chiefly affected in that sub-district being Southfield and Swaffield Road, and Streatham, where the schools chiefly affected were Oldridge Road and Mitcham Lane. There were also 201 cases notified from schools in Clapham the schools chiefly affected having been Telferscot Road and Cavendish Road. In Putney the school chiefly affected was Hotham Road, and in Tooting, Franciscan Road and Fountain Road. The greatest mortality from Measles and Whooping Cough occurs among children under five years of age. Of the 112 deaths that occurred from these diseases, 108 or 96.4 per cent. were deaths of children under five years. 96 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The following pamphlet is now left at every house where a case of Whooping Cough has been notified from school, and also in all other known cases. During the year 49 visits were made by the Sanitary Inspectors in cases of Whooping Cough, six in Clapham, 29 in Streatham, two in Tooting, and 12 in Wandsworth. The small number of cases visited is explained by the fact that it was only during the last six weeks of the year that these cases were systematically visited. "PRECAUTIONS AGAINST WHOOPING COUGH. "1.—Whooping Cough is a very fatal infectious disease. In the five years 1905-1909 the total number of deaths from Whooping Cough in London was 6,230, or nearly three times greater than the number of deaths from Scarlet Fever. "2.—It is very infectious, even before the well known crowing sound shows itself. "3.—When a child has Whooping Cough no other child or neighbour accompanied by a child must be admitted to the house, nor should the child who is ill be allowed to return to school or play with other children for 14 days after the <'whoops" have stopped altogether. "4.—A child suffering from Whooping Cough should if possible be kept in one room, with a fire burning in it and the window slightly open to admit fresh air. "5.—When one child has been attacked, the other children in the house who have not had the disease should be watched, and any illness accompanied by coughing should be regarded as probably Whooping Cough. "6.—All the other children in the house who have not bad Whooping Cough before must be kept away from school, but the other children living in the house who have had Whooping Cough may continue at school. "The same applies to the attendance of children at any public meeting such as Church, Sunday-school, or Children's Treat. 97 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "It is important to remember that children rarely take Whooping Cough more than once in their lives. "7.—The matter coughed or spat up by the patient should be received into a piece of clean paper or rag, and should at once be burned. All discharges from the nostrils should be treated in the same way. "8.—In all cases of Whooping Cough a doctor should be called in at once. "9.—When the cough has gone the rooms should be thoroughly cleansed. All the bed ticks, blankets and sheets, as well as all clothing which has been worn by the patient should be washed and well aired, in sunshine if possible. "P. Caldwell Smith, M.A., M.D., D.P.H., Camb., "Medical Officer of Health. "Public Health Department, "90, East Hill, Wandsworth, S.W. "July, 1910." Other Diseases Notified from Schools. No action is at present taken by the Sanitary Authority after notification of these diseases, although during the prevalence of Small-pox cases of Chicken-pox were visited. Epidemic Influenza. 55 deaths, 27 of males and 28 of females, were due to this disease, compared with 89 in 1909, 82 in 1908, 88 in 1907, and 70 in 1906. There has thus been a decrease of 34 deaths compared with the previous year, and of 28'8 compared with the corrected decennial average. 11 of the deaths occurred in Clapham, seven in Putney, 18 in Streatham, four in Tooting, and 15 in Wandsworth. The death-rate was .16 per 1,000, compared with .26 in 1909, .25 in 1908, .27 in 1907, and .22 in 1906. 25 of the deaths were of persons from 25 to 65 years, and 21 of persons over 65 years. Diarrhoea. 35 deaths, 22 of males and 13 of females, occurred from Diarrhoea and Zymotic Enteritis during the year, compared with 52 in 1909, 101 in 1908, 65 in 1907, 218 in 1906, 137 in 1905, and 208 in 1904. There has thus been a decrease of 17 in the number of deaths from these diseases compared with the previous year, and of 115, compared with the corrected decennial average. Three of the deaths occurred in Clapham, three in Putney, eight in Streatham, four in Tooting, and 17 in Wandsworth. In Clapham, Tooting and Wandsworth there was a decrease compared with 1909, and in Putney and Streatham a slight increase. 102 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In Clapham the number decreased from 10 to three, in Tooting from 14 to four, and in Wandsworth from 19 to 17, while in Putney the number increased from two to three, and in Streatham from seven to eight. The death-rate was .10 per 1,000, compared with .15 in 1909, .30 in 1908, .20 in 1907, and .70 in 1906. The death-rate for the whole of London also shows a decrease, it being .28, compared with .32 in 1909, but in the three previous years the rates were: .53 in 1908, .33 in 1907, and .95 in 1906. The death-rate in the sub-districts was as follows:—In Clapham .04, compared with .14 in 1909; in Putney 09, compared with .06; in Streatham .07, compared with '06; in Tooting 12, compared with .43; and in Wandsworth .17, compared with .19. Of the total number of deaths registered, 29 or 83 per cent. were of infants under one year, compared with 80 per cent. in 1909, 73 per cent. in 1908, 83 per cent. in 1907, 86 per cent. in 1906, and 87 per cent. in 1905. Including Enteritis, the total number of deaths was 88, compared with 124 in 1909, 183 in 1908, 127 in 1907, 289 in 1906, 203 in 1905, and 301 in 1904. Of these 88 deaths, 66 or 75 per cent. were of infants under one year. "We have the honour of submitting to you a report of our work among infants during the year 1910. "We had the help of seven voluntary workers at different times throughout the year—five for periods of three months each, viz.: March 1st to May 31st, March 9th to June 2nd, June 2nd to August 18th, June 8th to August 30th, and October 3rd to December 30th; one from September 14th to October 20th, and one from November 17th to December 22nd. With this additional help we were enabled to extend our work to every part of the Borough, as will be seen from the subjoined Tables. Out of the total number of visits, viz. : 6,874 (which included re-visits and ineffectual calls), 3,504 were paid by the voluntary workers (Clapham, North, 247; Clapham, South, 147; Putney 74, Streatham 281, Balham 246, Tooting 556, Southfield 467, Fairfield 170, Springfield 555, and 761 ineffectual visits). "The total number of visits paid after notification and registration of births will be found in Table LIV., which also shows the method of feeding at the first visit, and the number of ineffectual calls. "With regard to the Notification of Births Act, which came into force in this Borough in November, 1909, we have found considerable difference in the number of births notified in the various Wards. For instance, nearly every birth among the poorer classes in the Springfield Ward of Wandsworth was notified; consequently we were able to visit those infants at an earlier date than those in other parts. Bearing this in mind, we should like to draw your attention to the number of deaths in that Ward during the year, which you will see is higher than that of any other part of the Borough. The analysis of the causes of death we give below. "79 of the infants visited died during the year, their age in months at death being shown in Table LVI. In Clapham, North, five deaths occurred—two from Broncho-pneumonia, 108 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. one from Tubercular Meningitis, and two from Enteritis. In Clapham, South, one died from Convulsions, one (illegitimate) from Congestion of the Lungs, and one from Enteritis. In Putney, one (illegitimate) died from Acute Catarrh of Lungs, and one from Muco-Enteritis. In Streatham, seven died—two from Diarrhoea, and the others from Pneumonia, Whooping Cough, Tubercular Meningitis, Inanition and Prematurity, and Convulsions respectively. In Balham, eight died—three from Broncho-pneumonia, three from Diarrhoea, Gastritis and Enteritis, one from Marasmus, and one from Prematurity. In Tooting, nine died—four from Broncho-pneumonia (one being illegitimate), two from Enteritis (one illegitimate), one from Marasmus, one from Atrophy, and one from Tubercular Meningitis. In Southfield, 10 died—two from Enteritis, three (one illegitimate) from Diarrhoea, two from Bronchitis, one from Whooping Cough, and one from Prematurity and Inanition. In Fairfield, 10 died—five from Bronchitis, and the others from Prematurity and Inanition (a twin birth), Congenital Syphilis, Whooping Cough, Heart Failure, and Pyloric Stenosis respectively. In Springfield there were 26 deaths, mainly from Bronchitis and Pneumonia, 15 being due to that cause, one from Whooping Cough, one from Tuberculosis, the mother dying of Consumption within a few weeks of the child's death, one from Wasting, one from Peritonitis, one (illegitimate) from Syphilis, and eight (one illegitimate) from Diarrhoea, Enteritis and Gastritis—of these eight, four were in the Union Infirmary for some time, and died there, and one had also the complication of Acute Bronchitis. "Of the 3,488 infants visited 72 were premature (Clapham, North, eight; Clapham, South, one; Putney four, Streatham two, Balham two, Tooting six, Southfield 14, Fairfield 15 and Springfield 20); of these nine died (Clapham, North, one, Streatham, one, Balham one, Tooting one, Southfield one, Fairfield two). 26 were delicate from birth (Clapham, North, one; Clapham, South, one; Putney two, Streatham two, Balham three, Tooting four, Southfield one, Fairfield four, Springfield eight), and of these six died (Clapham, South, one; Streatham two, Tooting one, Fairfield one, Springfield one). 109 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "Table LV. shows the number of re-visits, and the method of feeding then found, the average age at the second visit being from four to five months. "The proportion of infants entirely breast fed this year is again practically the same as last, while the proportion of those having milk or food in addition to the breast is slightly higher. There is very little variation in the proportion of 110 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. those having cows' or condensed milk, but there is a slight increase in the use of farinaceous and other foods. This' applies to the method of feeding at the first visit. "With regard to the feeding at the second visit, there is a decrease in the proportion of those wholly breast fed, 62 as against 65 per cent., and a slight increase in the proportion of those having cows' milk only. On the other hand there is a decrease in the proportion of those having farinaceous and other foods, '69, as against i'i6 last year. The food given in addition to the breast is chiefly biscuit; very little bread-sop is now given, 'nursery biscuits' having taken its place. "The majority of the bottles used are of the boat shape, 776 of these being given (Clapham, North, 43; Clapham, South, 54; Putney 29, Streatham 80, Balham 112, Tooting 186, Southfield 85, Fairfield 70, Springfield 120), as against 132 tube bottles (Clapham, North, nine; Clapham, South, 14; Putney four, Streatham 15, Balham 33, Tooting 35, Southfield five, Fairfield five, Springfield 12). Both boat and tube bottles were in use in 20 cases (Clapham, South, three; Putney one, Streatham three, Balham seven, Tooting three, Fairfield one, Springfield two), and spoons in 134 (Clapham, North, three; Clapham, South, 15; Putney one, Streatham 17, Balham 19, Tooting 32, Southfield 18, Fairfield 11, Springfield 18). In 35 instances (Clapham, North, two; Clapham, South, five; Putney one, Streatham eight, Balham seven, Tooting five, Southfield two, Fairfield one, Springfield four), where tube bottles were being used at our first visit we found boat bottles substituted at the second visit. "We have this year in our talks with the mothers laid special stress on the dangers arising from the use of comforters or dummies, as we found the majority of babies were having them. In many instances the mother owned that the child refused to take it at first, but by dint of constantly putting it into the month the baby got used to it. In several cases the dummy was dipped into condensed milk before being given. 111 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "In only 792 cases were comforters not in use (Clapham, North, 73; Clapham, South, 22; Putney 35, Streatham 39, Balham 41, Tooting 61, Southfield 116, Fairfield 133, Springfield 272). In 90 instances where they were in use at the first visit they were discontinued at the second (Clapham, North, four; Clapham, South, seven; Putney six, Streatham four, Balham seven, Tooting 14, Southfield 10, Fairfield 12, Springfield 26). "With regard to the employment of midwives, out of the 3,488 mothers visited 1,342 availed themselves of their services. Of these, 1,073 employed midwives registered under the London County Council (Clapham, North, 64; Clapham, South, 48; Putney 63, Streatham 52, Balham 151, Tooting 223, Southfield 97, Fairfield 86, Springfield 289). The nonregistered midwives were employed chiefly by the very poor (Clapham, North, one; Clapham, South, 16; Putney 11, Streatham 26, Balham 37, Tooting 48, Southfield 36, Fairfield 46, Springfield 48), and during the early part of the year, as in accordance with the Midwives Act, they were prohibited from practising after April 1st, 1910. Any case of infringement of the Act discovered by us we have at once reported to you. "The sanitary defects found in the course of our work were remedied, or if involving structural work, referred to you. 200 intimation notices were served (Clapham, South, 14; Putney one, Streatham 17, Balham 32, Tooting 61, Southfield 11, Fairfield 25, Springfield 39), and 14 statutory notices were required (Balham two, Tooting seven, Southfield one, and Springfield four)." 112 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Of the 3,488 infants visited, it was found that 2,784, or 79'8. per cent., were wholly breast fed, while 704, or 20.2 per cent., were otherwise fed. If we add to the number wholly breast fed the number of children in which other foods were given in addition we find that the percentage is increased to 88.5, a slight decrease compared with the previous year. 235, or 6.7 per cent., were fed on cows' milk only, but 18 had other foods in addition, raising the percentage to 7.2, a decrease compared with 1908 and 1909, when the percentages were 7.2 and 6.4 respectively. 120 had condensed milk only, and 11 had condensed milk and food, a total of 131, or 3.7 per cent., an increase of .1 per cent. compared with 1909. 16 infants were fed on farinaceous and other foods, compared with eight in the previous year. Under the above Orders two cases of Anthrax and six cases of Glanders were notified by the Veterinary Inspectors of the London County Council. In these cases the necessary steps in connection with the disinfection and cleansing of the premises were taken by the officials of the London County Council, but the officers of the Borough Council visited the premises and obtained the names of any men who had been in contact with the diseased animals and notified them that if any symptoms of illness appeared the fact should be at once reported.
