The next Table shows the deaths and death rates in males and females from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, and for other Tubercular diseases, as well as the deaths and death-rates among •children under five years from other Tubercular diseases, for the 10 years 1900-1909, and for the year 1910. Among males the rate for Tubercular disease of the Lungs for the year 1910 was .98, compared with 1.16 the decennial average; and among females 59, compared with .74; while from 126 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. other Tubercular diseases the rate among males was .26, compared with .42; and among females .20, compared with .31. Among children under five years the rate for the year was .15, compared with .23, the decennial average. Although there has been a slight reduction in the rate for 1910, compared with the previous 10 years, yet this reduction will not be so great if the population has been overestimated, a possible error which can only be corrected after the publication of the Census returns for 1911. I am still of opinion that compulsory notification of all cases of this disease is necessary before any great reduction can occur, and also that some further action will require to be taken by Sanitary Authorities. At the present time the methods adopted by some Authorities are— (1) Sanatorium treatment. This can only deal with a few cases at a time, and which also has this objection, that it is a very expensive form of treatment. Among the well-to-do the curative value of sanatoria may be great, but this means prolonged stay in the sanatorium and residence under healthy conditions afterwards. Among the poor a prolonged stay is as a rule impossible, and, even if partially cured, a return to town life in many cases leads to a recrudescence of the disease. (2) Tuberculosis Dispensaries are now being established by several Sanitary Authorities, most of them on the lines of the Royal Victoria Dispensary for Consumption in Edinburgh, started about 20 years ago by Dr. R. W. Philip. In the Borough of Paddington, where a charitably supported dispensary was started in January, 1909, good work is being done, not only in examination of patients at the dispensary, but also in visiting the homes of the patients to arrange and supervise the treatment, and also to examine the other members of the family so that early cases may be at once detected and treated. 127 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 857 patients, 351 males and 506 females, were examined, and of these, 298, 126 males and 172 females, were definitely suffering from Phthisis. 295, 127 males and 168 females, were suspected to be suffering from other forms of Tuberculous diseases, while 249 were not Tuberculous. (3) Tuberculosis Dispensaries in which Tuberculin treatment in addition to home visiting is carried out. This is a comparatively new scheme for dealing with the disease, and has only recently been suggested. Treatment by Tuberculin promises good results as it is by far the cheapest method from an official point of view, and what is most important the patient, if in the initial stage of the disease, can continue at his ordinary work. It will be several years before any definite statistical results are available, showing the percentage of cure by this method, but so far as can be judged by the results already published, the percentage of cures is considerably greater than by any other method. A Tuberculosis Dispensary is being started in Portsmouth, and the results will be watched with interest by all those engaged in battling with this universal disease. It is in my opinion certain that in this Borough the establishment of a Tuberculosis Dispensary with Tuberculin treatment would be largely utilised, especially in the sub-districts of Wandsworth and Tooting, where, as far as can be ascertained, the incidence of the disease is greatest, and it is to be hoped that at no distant period some such institution, either charitably supported or rate supported, or both, will be available for consumptive patients. Compared with London as a whole the mortality in this Borough was .76 compared with 1.14, while the only other Boroughs in the Metropolis with a lower rate were Lewisham .52, and Hampstead .58 per 1,000. 128 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. One of the cases was an infant under one year, four from one to five years, 20 from five to 15 years, 43 from 15 to 25 years, 212 from 25 to 65 years, and 14 over 65 years. During the year notification of 22 cases of Consumption were received from the Medical Officer to the Education Department of the London County Council. These cases had been discovered in examination of children at schools. 134 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Of the 22 cases 15 had a definite family history, in one no information could be obtained, and in six no definite family history could be traced. Dr. Kerr in his report states that Tubercle as an evident cause of disease is not common in schools, in the form of pronounced Consumption being rare, not exceeding three per 1,000. He also remarks, however, that there are a large number of children in whom this disease is suspected. In France, Germany and Austria, where the children are tested with Tuberculin, the proportion of children in whom reaction is obtained is very large, and Dr. Kerr remarks that practically every child is infected by Tubercle, but these mostly get over it before school days are past. If this be the case in this country then it is a very strong argument for the establishment of a Tuberculosis Dispensary. The whole of the work in connection with these cases has been carried out by the female Sanitary Inspectors, and I am able to report that it was done in a most thorough and satisfactory manner. The total number of visits paid was 646, 122 in Clapham, 47 in Putney, 112 in Streatham, 79 in Tooting, and 286 in Wandsworth. As in previous years, the pamphlet which has been in use since 1903 was left, and the instructions thereon were in most of the cases carried out. During the year 12 sputum bottles have been supplied, and two were returned after the death of the patient. 135 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Of the 294 cases visited by the Inspectors it was possible to obtain a definite history of infection in 89. In five of the cases the father of the patient had died of Consumption, in 11 the mother, in four the sister, in five the husband, in seven the brother, in four the wife, in one the father and mother, in one father and sister, in six father and other relations, in four the mother and brother, in one the mother and other relation, in three the brother and sister, in three brother and other relation, and in one the sister and other relations. In three of the cases the family history was exceedingly bad, in one, four brothers, eight sisters and six other relations had died of Consumption; in one, the father, husband, brother, son, and another relation, and in one the father, two brothers, one sister, and two sons. In 103 of the cases no history of infection could be ascertained, in 47 no information could be obtained, and in 56 no inquiries were made. Of these 56, 19 were notified from Tooting Bee Asylum, 23 from the Workhouse, one from the Prison, one from Tooting Home, while in 11 the medical attendant did not wish any inquiries to be made. During the year disinfection was carried out at 265 premises, either after the death or removal of the patient, compared with 255 in 1909, 184 in 1908 and 135 in 1907. 56 of the premises disinfected were in Clapham, 25 in Putney, 56 in Streatham, 30 in Tooting, and 98 in Wandsworth. There has again been a marked increase in the number of deaths in Institutions, and to show that the increase has been going on for some years, the following Table has been prepared. The comparative percentages for London are also shown, and it will be noted that in the 20 years 1889-1908 these increased in London from 24.5 to 41.9 with an average of 31*14, and in Wandsworth from 15.2 to 28.7, with an average of 19.45. In this Borough the percentage is lower than for the whole of London, and this is what might be expected in consequence of the social condition of the population, but in spite of the better social condition, it is rather startling to find that over 30 per cent. of the total deaths occurred in Institutions, and that for the year 1909 16 per cent. occurred in the Workhouse and Workhouse Infirmary. Compared with the 20 years' average, the greatest increase has occurred among the deaths in Workhouse and Workhouse Infirmaries, while the rate in the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals is slightly lower. Cutting-off Notices. During the year 76 notices with respect to 104 premises were received from the Water Board stating that the supply had been withdrawn. 15 of the premises were in Clapham, 19 in Putney, 31 in Streatham, 15 in Tooting, and 24 in Wandsworth. The supply was withdrawn for non-payment of rates from 46 premises, from 23 premises in consequence of defects, in 20 the premises were empty, in 13 the supply was withdrawn by request, and in two the reason was not stated. Compared with 1909, there has been a decrease in the number of notices received, chiefly in consequence of defects and at the request of the owners. Mortuaries. The Mortuaries in Clapham and Streatham have been visited several times during the year, and I am able to report that the By-laws and Regulations have been carried out in a satisfactory manner. With reference to the Wandsworth Mortuary the Public Health Committee has had the question of providing a new mortuary for Wandsworth before them, but so far no decision as to the site has been arrived at. The Mortuary at Tooting has been closed, but a temporary one has been provided pending the erection of a new one in Wandsworth. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 151 In Clapham, 21 bodies were removed to the Mortuary, compared with 38 in 1909; in Streatham 56, compared with 55; and in Wandsworth 109, of which 60 were from Wandsworth, 33 from Putney, and 16 from Tooting-—a total of 186, compared with 206 in 1909. General Sanitary Work in 1910. The following list shows the special inspections made by me during the year :— (1.) In consequence of several complaints of flooding from the River Wandle, special inspections were made on several occasions during the year of the whole course of the River from the Borough boundary to the River Thames, but it was found that only at one part of the River was there any flooding of occupied houses, viz. : in Lydden Grove. The attention of the owners was called to the matter, and the London County Council was also informed, but owing to the absence of heavy rainfall the flooding did not recur. If the flooding does recur it will be necessary for the owners to construct a proper retaining wall. (2.) As in previous years, special inspections were made of several localities in consequence of complaints of smells from road gullies and sewer ventilators. The number of complaints was greater than in the previous year, especially in Wandsworth ; this having been due to the fact that the climatic conditions were favourable. In all 117 complaints were received, compared with 79 in 1909: six from Clapham North, five from Clapham South, 22 from Putney, 18 from Streatham, 16 from Balham, 15 from Tooting, 21 from Southfield, and 14 from Springfield Wards. 152 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. All these complaints were forwarded to the Borough Engineer and Surveyor, who reported that in every case the necessary cleansing and flushing or other work had been carried out. (3.) A large number of inspections were again made of passages and mews, and a considerable number of these have been satisfactorily dealt with during the year, but in some, owing to legal difficulties as to ownership, no work has yet been begun to comply with the notices served. Special house-to-house inspections were made during the year in Young's Rents, Rowland's Cottages, Wirtemberg Street, North Street, and Manor Street, Clapham; Bigg's Row, Labourers' Cottages, and Brewhouse Lane, Putney; Sunnyhill Road, Wellfield Road, Edgington Road, Hazelhurst Road and Foss Road, Streatham; Salvador and Shepherd's Close, Tooting; and King's Arms Yard, Stockwell Court, Marters Cottages, Cumbers Yard, Point Pleasant, and Ram Square, Wandsworth. These inspections were made chiefly on account of the damp conditions reported to me by the Sanitary Inspectors, and also to ascertain if any of these premises could be reported to the Council under the Housing, Town Planning, etc., Act. Including the house-to-house inspections and inspections for certificates under the Revenue Act, but excluding the routine inspection of bakehouses, slaughter-houses, cowsheds, and offensive trade premises, 1,310 inspections were made of premises, chiefly previous to the service of statutory notices. My attendance at the Police Court was necessary on 16, at the Sessions on one, and at the County Court on two occasions. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 155 Revenue Act. During the year 14 certificates were granted under the above Act, one was deferred, and two refused. The certificates granted referred to 73 houses, or 147 tenements. Housing, Town Planning, &c., Act, 1909. In this Borough, where the great majority of the houses are comparatively new, the number of houses which at the present could be dealt with under the above Act is very small, as an inspection of the whole Borough shows. There are, however, some small properties in the older parts where it was necessary to take action, and five houses were closed under Section 17 of the above Act. Four of these houses were situated in Putney and one in Wandsworth, and after reporting on the matter to the Public Health Committee the premises were closed by the owner after notice. Nothing has so far been done by the owner to render the houses fit for habitation. The question of taking action under Section 18 of the Act will be considered by the Committee in the current year. Three other houses, in Clapham, were reported on, but the Committee after inspecting the houses were of opinion that they could be dealt with under the Public Health (London) Act, and notices were served under that Act, which were complied with. 156 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Sanitary Operations. The Table of Sanitary operations shows in detail the amount and nature of the work carried out by the Sanitary Inspectors during the year. The number of complaints received and attended to was 1,789, compared with 1,692 in 1909, 1,645 in 1908, and 1,836 in 1907. I here was an increase in Putney, Streatham, Balham, Tooting, Southfield, Fairfield and Springfield Wards, and a decrease in Clapham North and South Wards. Seven of these complaints were made to the London County Council, and these were at once attended to. The number of houses and premises inspected was 29,931, an increase of 3,096 compared with 1909, and of 5,039 compared with 1908. There was an increase in the number of houses and premises inspected in Clapham North and South, Putney, Streatham, Balham, Tooting, Southfield and Springfield Wards, and a decrease in Fairfield Ward. The total number of house-to-house inspections made was 6,276, compared with 7,007 in 1909, 5,575 in 1908, 5,718 in 1907, 5,686 in 1906, and 6,290 in 1905. There has been an increase in the number of house-to-house inspections in Putney, Tooting, and Southfield Wards, and a decrease in Clapham North and South, Streatham, Balham, Fairfield, and Springfield Wards. In Tooting alone the number increased from 226 in 1908 and 769 in 1909, to 850 in 1910, and in Putney from 649 in 1909 to 806 in 1910. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 157 The principal reason why the number of house-to-house inspections is lower is due to the fact that the whole system of recording these inspections was in the last three months of the year re-organised, as it was found that the records previously kept did not contain all the matters dealt with by the Housing, Town Planning, etc., Act. After much consideration it was decided to adopt a card system of recording these inspections, and cards were drawn up so that the result of the inspection could be entered by the Sanitary Inspector at the time of making his house-to-house inspection. The cards are, after completion by the Inspector, filed according to streets, thus making the work of future reference an easy matter. Although the work of inspecting a house and the entering of all the information at the time occupies more of the time of the Inspector yet the system will, in my opinion, prove a more complete and satisfactory one, and it will also save a large amount of clerical work which had to be performed at the Office. In 3,920 out of a total of 6,276 houses inspected, or 62.46 per cent., sanitary defects necessitating the service of an intimation notice were found. The highest percentage was in the Streatham Ward, where the percentage was 85.84, and the lowest in Clapham South, where it was only 54.24, the next lowest being Clapham North Ward, where it was 55.05. The greatest number of intimation notices was served in Southfield and Springfield Wards, and the smallest number in Fairfield and Putney Wards. The greatest number of statutory notices was served in Balham and Springfield Wards, and the smallest number in Putney and Clapham South Wards. 126 of the statutory notices, 21 in Clapham, six in Putney, 32 in Streatham, six in Tooting, and 61 in Wandsworth, served in 1910, were not complied with at the close of the year. These notices were served in November and December, and with the exception of 18 premises which were vacated after service of the notices, and of 30 which were in hand, were complied with in the early months of the current year. Of the 30 in hand the majority were notices for paving yards or open spaces which could not be complied with owing to climatic conditions. The number of statutory notices served include By-law notices, 394 of which were served in respect of the following contraventions of the By-laws:—Yard paving 179, repairs to water closets 38, repairs to flushing apparatus of water closets 49, cleansing of water closets two, covers to cisterns 16, cleansing cisterns 11, cleansing house and other contraventions of the By-laws as to houses let in lodgings 73, paving and draining stables one, absence of cover to manure receptacles seven, paving of pig sties one, and failing to deposit manure in a proper receptacle 17. The number of cases of overcrowding dealt with during the year was 129, compared with 131 in 1909, 158 in 1908, 87 in 1907, 100 in 1906, and 92 in 1905. Seven of these were in Clapham, three in Putney, 18 in Streatham, 13 in Tooting, and 88 in Wandsworth. 174 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The cases of overcrowding in Clapham were chiefly in Crichton Street; in Putney, in Quill Lane; in Streatham, in Larch Road, Zennor Road, and Moffat Road; in Tooting, in Fountain Road; and in Wandsworth, in Guelph Street, Lydden Road, Bendon Valley, and Lydden Grove. Nearly all the cases were caused by sub-letting rooms, largely in consequence of the original occupier being out of employment. All the cases were remedied without difficulty, and it was not necessary to take any Police Court proceedings. 1,060 ashpits were provided or repaired, compared with 1,292 in 1909, and 336 complaints with reference to the non-removal of house refuse (66 from Clapham, 41 from Putney, 99 from Streatham, 14 from Tooting, and 116 from Wandsworth) were received during the year. These complaints were, as in previous years, attended to by the Dusting Inspectors. 1,437 yards in connection with dwelling-houses, and 97 in connection with stables, were paved, drained, or cleansed, compared with 1,574 ancl 95 in 1909. 6,010 rooms and 1,266 staircases and passages were cleansed or repaired, compared with 7,301 rooms and 1,519 staircases and passages in 1909. The greatest number of rooms cleansed was in the Balham Ward, where 1,163 were done, and in Springfield Ward, where 1,060 were done, and the smallest number in Fairfield and Putney, where the numbers were 285 and 287 respectively. 1,084 drains were cleansed or repaired, and 64 rain-water pipes disconnected from the drains. 114 notices of reconstruction of drains were sent to the Borough Engineer from this Department: 37 in Clapham, compared with 34 in 1909; seven in Putney, compared with five; 36 in Streatham, compared with 51; 13 in Tooting, compared with 14; and 21 in Wandsworth, compared with 40. 175 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The total number of houses where the drains were reconstructed during the year was 280: 65 in Clapham, 19 in Putney, 101 in Streatham, 18 in Tooting-, and 85 in Wandsworth. 361 accumulations of manure were removed after service of notice, compared with 347 in 1909: 78 in Clapham, 46 in Putney, 130 in Streatham, 21 in Tooting, and 86 in Wandsworth. There has been an increase in the number of cases dealt with in Clapham, Putney and Streatham, and a decrease in Tooting and Wandsworth. 38 notices were served for the periodical removal of manure every 48 hours, compared with 50 in 1909 and 55 in 1908. The number of waterclosets cleansed and repaired was 2,149, compared with 2,128 in 1909. The greatest number of these were in Balham and Springfield Wards. 606 drinking water cisterns were cleansed, covered, or repaired: 245 in Clapham, 47 in Putney, 122 in Streatham, 52 in Tooting, and 140 in Wandsworth, compared with 837 in 1909. 138 premises were inspected and notices served where animals were kept in such a manner as to be a nuisance. Five of these were in Clapham, four in Putney, 37 in Streatham, 12 in Tooting, and 80 in Wandsworth. This nuisance was most common in Southfield, Fairfield and Springfield Wards, and the animals kept were chiefly ducks and hens. 68 smoke nuisances were reported during the year: two in Clapham, three in Putney, five in Streatham, and 58 in Wandsworth. Three complaints of black smoke were received from the Public Control Department of the London County Council with regard to three premises, and five complaints from the Coal Smoke Abatement Society with regard to six premises. 176 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. These complaints were all at once attended to and the premises kept under observation. In all, 220 observations for black smoke were made by the Sanitary Inspectors. 68 cases were reported, and 62 notices were served. 153 soil pipes were repaired, 28 in Clapham, 10 in Putney, 43 in Streatham, 57 in Tooting, and 15 in Wandsworth, compared with 182 in 1909 and 125 in 1908. 1,225 leaky roofs and gutters were repaired, compared with 1,159 in 1909 and 945 in 1908. 38 unwholesome or dilapidated houses were cleansed and repaired, four in Clapham, two in Putney, six in Streatham, 10 in Tooting, and 16 in Wandsworth, compared with 46 in 1909. A large amount of work has again been carried out to abate nuisances arising from damp conditions. In all 1,754 premises have been dealt with, 160 in Clapham, 84 in Putney, 520 in Streatham, 192 in Tooting, and 698 in Wandsworth; and in 634 of these, 38 in Clapham, 11 in Putney, 188 in Streatham, 47 in Tooting, and 350 in Wandsworth, damp courses have been Inserted. The number of unclassified nuisances was 746, 262 in Clapbam, 63 in Putney, 248 in Streatham, 41 in Tooting, and 132 in Wandsworth, compared with 719 in 1909. Disinfection. The total number of rooms disinfected after the occurrence of cases of infectious disease was 2,166, (390 in Clapham, 250 in Putney, 564 in Streatham, 448 in Tooting, and 514 in Wandsworth), compared with 3,146 in 1909, 2,868 in 1908, 3,206 in 1907, and 2,794 in 1906. 177 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The decrease was chiefly due to the decrease in the number .of disinfections carried out after Measles, Scarlet Fever and Diphtheria. 1,230 lots of bedding were stoved at the Disinfecting- Station, .compared with 1,584 in 1909, 1,826 in 1908, 1,948 in 1907, and 1,669 in 1906. The 1,230 lots of bedding included the following:—694 beds, 755 bolsters, 1,916 pillows, 743 mattresses, 341 palliasses, 1,033 sheets, 1,166 quilts, 519 cushions, 4,162 articles of clothing, 2,062 blankets, 168 carpets, and 1,871 extra articles, a total of 15,430 articles, compared with 20,843 in 1909, 24,034 in 1908, 29,102 in 1907, and 28,885 in 1906. 418 premises were disinfected after Measles and 265 after Tubercular disease of the Lungs, compared with 1,012 and 255 in 1909. 27 beds, 11 bolsters, 53 pillows, 44 mattresses, 53 palliasses, 10 sheets, 12 quilts, 20 cushions, 69 articles of clothing, 13 blankets, 17 carpets, and 109 extra articles, were removed and destroyed by request of the owners during the year. Disinfection was also carried out by the Sanitary Staff at premises after non-notifiable diseases, chiefly Cancer, and the sum of £20 7s. od. was received for such disinfection. 277 rooms were disinfected with sulphur in consequence of their verminous condition, and 10 lots of verminous bedding .disinfected. I have regularly visited the Disinfecting Station during the year, and am able to report that the work has been carried out by the staff to my entire satisfaction. N 178 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Only two complaints of damage to goods by disinfection have been received, and on inquiry it was found that the goods were damaged or stained before being placed in the disinfecting chamber. The baths at the Disinfecting Station have not been so frequently used this year, chiefly owing to the smaller number of verminous children dealt with. The names of 116 children, 10 belonging to Clapham, six to Putney, 16 to Streatham, 26 to Tooting, and 58 to Wandsworth, stated to be in a verminous condition, were forwarded to me by 179 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. the Medical Officer, Education Department, London County Council, and the homes were visited by the Inspectors. In the majority of cases only the heads of the children were verminous, but in a few cases the bodies and clothing were also verminous. Of these latter, four, two boys and two girls, were cleansed at the baths, and the clothing was at the same time stoved with beneficial results. 15 adults also had baths, five being nurses who had been in attendance on cases of Puerperal Fever, five were contacts with cases of Scarlet Fever or Diphtheria, while five, three females and two males, were verminous. Inspection of Urinals in connection with Licensed Premises. The number of urinals inspected was 149, 33 in Clapham, 20 in Putney, 25 in Streatham, 11 in Tooting, and 60 in Wandsworth. 324 inspections were made during the year and 38 notices, chiefly for cleansing, were served and complied with. Police Court Proceedings. The list which follows shows particulars of Police Court proceedings under the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, during the year. The total amount of fines inflicted was £23 4s. od., and the costs £14 7s., compared with £30 17s. 6d. and £11 3s. od. in 1909. N 2 180 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The majority of the cases were with respect to nuisances or . contraventions of By-laws, while four were on account of the absence of a proper supply of water. During the year ending 31st March, 1910, an alteration has been made in the system of removal. The whole of the refuse is now collected and disposed of by contract, with the exception of a portion from the Balham, Streatham and Tooting Wards, which is disposed of at the Destructor. Mortuaries— Total number of bodies removed 186 Total number of infectious bodies removed— Milkshops. At the close of 1909 there were 331 milkshops on the Register, 36 have been added in 1910 and 31 removed, leaving 336 on the Register at the end of 1910. During the year 995 inspections were made of these premises and 44 notices, chiefly for cleansing, were served. Five prosecutions were instituted for carrying on the business of a dairyman without being registered, and fines amounting to £5 were imposed, with £.2 15s. costs. The safes provided by purveyors of milk who also sell general goods, have proved very satisfactory in preventing the pollution of the milk, and these purveyors are still of opinion that their provision has effected a decided improvement. There were 18 Slaughter-houses and one Knacker's Yard on the Register in 1909, one in Streatham was removed, leaving 18 on the Register. During the year 457 inspections were made of these, but it was only necessary to serve four notices for cleansing. During 1910 28 were added and 15 removed, leaving a total of 287 on the Register. 427 inspections were made of these premises during the year and 51 notices were served, all of which were for cleansing. Houses Let in Lodgings. At the end of 1909 there were 275 houses let in lodgings on the Register. 17 were added during 1910 and none removed, making a total of 292 on the Register at the end of 1910. 790 inspections of these were made during the year, and 511 notices were served, 59 for overcrowding and 452 for other conditions, chiefly cleansing. With regard to one premises a summons was issued for failing to cleanse, and a conviction was obtained. On the whole the By-laws have been satisfactorily complied with, although in some instances the cleansing was not carried out at the specified time. 191 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Offensive Trade Refuse. No complaints have been received during the year as to the removal of the above refuse, and I am able to report that the arrangement entered into by the Council has been satisfactorily carried out by the Contractor. Unsound Food. During the year, 10 cases of Haddocks, two trunks of Whiting, four boxes of Herrings, one box of Plaice, four boxes of Skate, 6 boxes of Cod, one box Winkles, one trunk of Soles, one box Skate, 29 Hake, 30 Mackerel, 120 lbs. Rock Salmon, 50 boxes of Tomatoes, 5 pecks of Strawberries, 36 Melons, 217 lbs. Beef and 12 Turkeys, were destroyed at the request of the owners, and the usual certificates granted. Factories and Workshops. Although the total number of inspections of factories, workshops and workplaces is less than in 1909, yet a very large amount of work was carried out by the male and female Inspectors during the year. This decrease was chiefly due to the smaller number of inspections of other workshops and workplaces. I am able to report that the condition of all the factories, workshops and workplaces is satisfactory, and that no difficulty has, during the year, been experienced in having the necessary cleansing and other sanitary work carried out by the occupiers. As in former years there has been some slight difficulty in obtaining the lists of outworkers, and it has been found necessary to send a circular letter twice in each year to the majority of employers, otherwise the lists would not have been forwarded. 192 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. This circular letter in all cases was successful in its object, and it was not necessary to take further steps. In nearly all the cases the official forms are now used, thus facilitating the clerical work and filing of the documents. The Tables which follow are similar to those used in previous Reports, and the whole of the work done by both male and female Inspectors is there shown in a concise form. The total number of workshops on the Register was 1,707, including 136 retail bakehouses, 153 workshop laundries, 302 dressmakers' and milliners' premises, and 1,116 other workshops, but not including 360 factories, 1,039 workplaces, and 314 outworkers' premises. Including all factories, workshops, workplaces, and outworkers' premises, there were on the Register at the end of 1910 3,420 premises, compared with 3,305 in 1909, 3,347 in 1908, 3,281 in 1907, 3,096 in 1906, 3,045 in 1905, 2,987 in 1904, and 2,676 in 1903. The total number of inspections made during the year was 6,387, compared with 6,531 in 1909, 5,801 in 1908, 6,048 in 1907, 5,997 in 1906, 4,933 in 1905, and 4,946 in 1904. The total number of notices served was 580, 544 intimation and 36 statutory, compared with 597 in 1909, 434 in 1908, 453 in 1907, 427 in 1906, 462 in 1905, and 465 in 1904. There were only two prosecutions under the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, both for sanitary defects. Under the head of other workshops are included domestic workshops, and under the head of places where food is prepared for sale are included the kitchens of hotels, restaurants, tripe, fried fish and eel shops, and other places where food is prepared for sale. 193 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In [all 725 inspections were made of 360 factories, and 61 notices, 59 imitation and two statutory, were served. The total number of workshops on the Register was 1,707, 153 of which were workshop laundries, 136 workshop bakehouses, and 1,418 other workshops. In all 3,346 inspections of these were made, compared with .3,366 in 1909, 3,130 in 1908, 3,148 in 1907, 3,233 in 1906, and 2,628 in 1905, and 252 intimation and 22 statutory notices were served. The total number of workplaces on the Register was 1,039, and 1,828 inspections were made, while 197 intimation and 10 statutory notices were served. The total number of defects found was 720, including 11 cases in which there was a breach of special sanitary requirements for bakehouses, compared with 745 and six in 1909. To abate these nuisances 544 intimation and 36 statutory notices were served, all of which, with the exception of 16, were complied with at the end of the year. The 16 outstanding were complied with in the first month of this year. In all, 720 defects were found and 704 remedied, but only 544 intimation notices were served, as one notice frequently called attention to several defects on the same premises. 194 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The table which follows and which relates to Home Work is similar to the one in last year's Report. There has been a decrease of 20 in the number of lists sent in twice a year, and of two in the number sent in once a year. 197 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In all, 132 lists were received twice in the year, containing the names and addresses of 83 contractors and 376 workers, and four lists once in the year, containing the names and addresses of 10 workers. There has been a very great reduction in the number of outworkers employed by employers in the Borough. The total number of addresses received from other Councils was 422, compared with 370 in 1909, 352 in 1908, 322 in 1907, and 173 in 1906, and 253 addresses were forwarded to other Councils, campared with 334 in 1909, 301 in 1908, 325 in 1907, and 122 in 1906. The number of addresses received has increased by 52, and the number sent decreased by 81. The following are the districts to which lists of outworkers were forwarded, and also the number of outworkers:—Lambeth 53, Stepney 44, Battersea 42, Southwark 22, City of London 19, Fulham 15, Merton nine, Camberwell six, Hammersmith five,. St. Pancras, Croydon, Chelsea, and Westmister three each, Bethnal Green, Southampton, Leyton, Islington, Woolwich, Lewisham, Hove, Bermondsey and Stoke Newington two each, West Ham, Acton, Wimbledon, Willesden, Twickenham, Shoreditch, East Ham and Finsbury one each. 94 names and addresses were forwarded to this Borough in error, and are not included in the above Table. The number of notices sent to employers during the year was 100, compared with 127 in 1909, and these had a most satisfactory result, although in a few cases it was also necessary for the Inspector to call and warn them that if such lists were not sent proceedings would be taken. At the end of 1909 there were 312 outworkers' premises on the Register, 73 were added and 71 removed during the year, leaving 314 on the Register. 488 inspections were made of these premises during the year, and 40 notices, all for cleansing, were served and complied with. 198 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. During the year 14 cases of infectious disease, six of Scarlet Fever, five of Diphtheria, two of Erysipelas, and one of Enteric Fever, were notified as having occurred in the homes of outworkers, compared with six in 1909, 22 in 1908, six in 1907, six in 1906, and five in 1905. The total number of inspections was 2,768, compared with 3,175 in 1909, 2,263 in 1908, 2,408 in 1907 and 1,964 in 1906, and re-inspections 1,534, compared with 1,184, 1,394, and 1,649, a total of 4,302, compared with 4,359 in the previous year. 848 of the inspections and 226 of the re-inspections were in Clapham, 226 and 289 in Putney, 667 and 406 in Streatham, 229 and 125 in Tooting, and 798 and 465 in Wandsworth. Inspector, the majority of these having been previously entered on the Register. In all the workshops under the supervision of the male Inspectors only one case of overcrowding was discovered, and this was at once remedied. 202 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The Tables which follow summarises the work done during the year by the female Sanitary Inspectors. The total number of premises inspected was 1,837 (403 in Clapham, 240 in Putney, 465 in Streatham, 181 in Tooting, and 518 in Wandsworth). 173 inspections were made of factories, 1,000 of workshops, 66 of domestic workshops, 373 of workplaces, and 225 of outworkers' premises, while 236 premises were visited for inquiry and no workroom found. There was only one case of overcrowding discovered, compared with two in 1909, four in 1908, four in 1907, five in 1906, 12 in 1905, and 17 in 1904, and this was at once abated. 30 were found to be in a dirty condition, compared with 56 in 1909, 25 in 1908, and 66 in 1907. In every workshop inspected during the year there were separate water closets for the sexes, but in five factories and four workshops the water closets were defective. Other nuisances were discovered in four factories, 25 workshops, one domestic workshop, seven workplaces, and five outworkers' premises, and these were all abated, with three exceptions, and these were abated in January of the current year. It was only necessary to serve 76 intimation notices, and these were all complied with with the exception of three, one in Streatham and two in Tooting. These notices were complied with .in the beginning of the current year. It was necessary to serve four statutory notices during the year, two in Clapham and two in Putney, and these were all complied with. 49 workshops and seven domestic workshops where females were employed were reported to H.M. Inspectors of Workshops, and 83 were reported to this Council by H.M. Inspector. Only 11 of the workshops (two in Clapham, seven in Streatham, one in Tooting, and one in Wandsworth), reported by H.M. Inspector were unknown to the Sanitary Inspectors. The other 72 had been previously reported to H.M. Inspector, or were already on the Register. 2o6 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. During the past year 1,060 samples of foods and drugs were .submitted to the Public Analyst for analysis, compared with 1,030 in 1909, 1,004 in 1908, 989 in 1907, and 905 in 1906. Of these, 96 or 9.05 per cent. were reported to be adulterated, compared with 6.8g per cent. in 1909, 8.76 per cent. in 1908, 8.49 per cent. in 1907, and 9.29 percent, in 1906. The percentage of milk samples found to be adulterated was 7.44, compared with 7.39 in 1909, 6.9 in 1908, 10.97 in 1907, and 10.26 in 1906. In 21 of the adulterated milk samples the Public Health Committee, after considering the amount of adulteration and the circumstances of the sale, decided not to prosecute the vendor, so that the percentages of prosecutions was only 3.9, compared with 4 per cent. in 1909, 3.8 in 1908, and 5.9 in 1907. In 55 out of the 96 cases in which adulterations were detected proceedings were instituted against the vendor, compared with 44 in 1909, and 50 in 1908. The total amount received in penalties under the aDove Acts during the year amounted to £115 15s. 6d., and the costs to £38 15s. 6d., making a total of £154 iis. od., compared with £g2 5s. od. in 1909, and .£118 9s. 6d. in 1908. 21$ Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Table CI. shows the number of samples taken, the number adulterated, and the percentage of adulterated samples for the years 1900-1909, compared with the year 1910. As will be seen from the above Table the percentage of adulterated samples detected during the 10 years 1900-1909 was 9.17, compared with 9.05 in 1910, showing a decrease of .12 per cent., but an increase of 2.16 per cent. compared with 1909. Although the percentage of adulterated samples shows this large increase compared with 1909, it must not be concluded that the food supply of the Borough has deteriorated to that extent. Such an increase can be accounted for in several ways, but is mainly due to discrimination in the selection of, and the methods adopted in obtaining, the samples for analysis. In all, 591 samples of milk and two of cream were taken. 44 of the samples of the milk and one of the cream, or 7.5 per cent. were found to be adulterated. Of the total 593, 540 were samples purchased by the Inspector, of which 40 or 7.4 per cent, were adulterated, while there were purchased by his deputy 53, of which five or 9.4 per cent. were adulterated. The one sample of cream which was found to be adulterated contained 20 grains of crystallised boric acid per pound. In this case proceedings were taken and a fine of 20s. was inflicted with 12 s. 6d. costs. 219 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 17 samples of milk were taken in course of delivery at the various Public Institutions in the Borough. Three samples were reported against as being adulterated. In two of the three cases the vendors were warned, and in the remaining case proceedings were taken against the vendor who, however, successfully proved a warranty. 28 samples of milk were taken at the various railway stations in the Borough, the milk being in course of delivery from the farmer to the retail vendor. On the whole this milk was exceptionally good, in most cases containing upwards of 4 per cent. of milk fat, and in no case was any adulteration detected. Nine samples of milk were taken on delivery at private houses, four of these being taken by the Inspector from the small can containing the early morning supply as it was being placed on the doorstep by the vendor. Three of the four taken in this way proved to be adulterated. In one case the Inspector had the vendor under observation for a considerable period, and arrangements were made to take a sample from him immediately after leaving his shop for the early morning round. The Inspector, who concealed himself in the immediate vicinity, saw the vendor leave his shop and serve several customers, leaving the milk in a small can on the doorstep. The Inspector then approached the vendor and demanded a sample from the can from which the customers had been served. This the vendor refused to sell, saying that he must mix it with the milk in his churn before sampling. The Inspector protested against this method, but the vendor insisted on mixing the milk. A sample taken from the milk when mixed proved an analysis to contain 4 per cent, of added water. At the same time a sample was taken from one of the small cans left on the doorstep, which proved on analysis to contain 35 per cent, of added water. Proceedings were taken against the vendor, who was convicted and fined £3 with £.2 6s. costs. 220 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In another case the vendor had flooded the district with handbills stating- that he could supply "Pure new milk" at 3d. per quart, of the same quality as was being supplied by local men at 4d. per quart. The vendor canvassed the district for custom, and once the customers were secured the fraud began by the substitution of skimmed milk for new milk. The Inspector had to watch his opportunity, and during the dark hours of early morning saw the vendor in the act of leaving some milk on the doorstep of one of his victims. He then went up quickly and, taking hold of the can, informed the vendor that the milk would be submitted for analysis by the Public Analyst. This sample proved to be deficient in milk fat to the extent of 31 per cent. Proceedings followed and the vendor was convicted and fined ^5 with £2 costs. The third case occurred on a Sunday morning, the Inspector having procured a sample for analysis, disclosed his identity, whereupon the vendor (knowing the milk was well watered) seized the jug and upset the contents, refusing to serve another sample. Proceedings were then taken for wilful obstruction and refusing to sell, and a fine of .£"10 and costs or one month's imprisonment being imposed, but the defendant having no money or goods went to prison for one month. TABLE CIV. Premises. No. of Samples taken. No. Adulterated. Percentage Adulterated. Public Institutions 17 3 18 Railway Stations 28 ... ... Private Houses 9 3 33 164 samples of milk and 10 of butter were procured during the early morning and mid-day of Sundays. Of these 15 samples of milk proved to be adulterated, a percentage of 9.1, compared with 2.6 in 1909, and 9 in 1908. 221 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 220 samples of milk were procured during the early morning' on week days and several samples were also taken from wholesale ffirms when delivering milk to retailers between the hours of 3 a.m. and 5 a.m. In five cases proceedings were taken against vendors for selling milk from receptacles on which their name and address was not conspicuously inscribed, and in each case a penalty was inflicted. In four cases proceedings were taken against vendors for wilful obstruction, and for refusing to sell, the penalties inflicted being £.2 and costs, £3 and costs, £2 and costs or 21 days imprisonment, £10 and costs or one month's imprisonment. In five cases summonses were dismissed owing to the defendant pleading the warranty clause. As I have repeatedly mentioned in my Reports this method of defence is a very serious drawback to the effective working of the Acts, and every effort should be made by those in authority to procure the abolition or amendment of this clause. The Inspector has on many occasions during the year reported that it is a most difficult matter to deal with the itinerant vendor of separated milk under the existing law, and while this defect continues the number of this class of vendor will tend to increase. They do not, however, all escape justice, for during the year no less than eight of this class of vendor were convicted, the fines ranging from £1 to £10 and costs, and in two cases the defendants were sent to prison. The following Table shows the number of milk vendors who are not actually ratepayers in this Borough, but who carry on trade in various parts of the Borough. The Table also shows the number of samples taken, the number adulterated, as well as the number and percentage of prosecutions. 222 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. It will be noticed that the percentage of prosecutions of those traders residing outside the Borough is almost double that of those residing within the Borough. TABLE CV. Milk vendors having registered premises within the Borough. Milk vendors from surrounding districts trading within the Borough. No. of Samples taken. No. adulterated. Percentage of adulterations. No. in which proceedings were taken. No. of Samples taken. No. adulterated. Percentage of adulterations. No. in which proceedings were taken. 410 26 63 11 or 38 per cent. 182 18 10 11 or 61 per cent. Butter. 304 samples of butter were submitted to the Public Analyst for analysis, most of the samples having been purchased during the busy shopping hours in the evening. 22 samples were reported against as being either wholly margarine or a mixture of margarine and butter, and in each case proceedings were taken and penalties inflicted. Four of the samples of butter reported against as being margarine were taken by the Inspector as butter, being exposed for sale by retail. The following instance will show how the public can be defrauded. The Inspector, seeing that a block of butter exposed an a marble slab was composed of two distinct colours, asked the vendor for a sample. The vendor in serving the Inspector scooped out a piece from the top of the block. The Inspector refused to take this, and asked for a piece to be taken off containing the 223 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. two colours, and after some hesitation the vendor did as requested, placing the piece on the slab and chopping off the darker portion of the piece, which afterwards proved to be margarine. The Inspector, not being satisfied with the method adopted by the vendor, procured a sample by cutting off a slice, sufficient for analysis, which coutaiued the two substances, and this on analysis proved to be equal parts of butter and margarine. It was obvious that this substance was being sold as butter, and being so placed it was within the power of the vendor to manipulate the proportion of margarine and butter according to the customer. The defendant, who was ably defended, was convicted and fined £5 and costs. It is satisfactory to note that in no case during the year has any sample of butter been reported against owing to excess of moisture, or to the presence of a preservative. Cheese. Five samples of gorgonzola cheese were taken, and in four of them it was found that the rind contained a certain amount of barytes and tallow, varying from 15 to 25 per cent. The vendors in these cases were warned and no proceedings were taken. Lard. During the year a memorandum was received from the Local Government Board drawing attention to the sale of lard substitutes as lard. The memorandum stated that it is desirable to direct special attention to the sale of this article, on account of the increase which has lately taken place in the importation of lard substitutes into this country, and of the marked difference in the price of these substitutes compared with genuine lard. 224 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The memorandum pointed out that samples of lard substitutes have been found to contain as much as 24 per cent. of moisture, and that samples taken in the North of England were found to contain solid paraffin. I therefore instructed the Inspector to take samples of lard in various parts of the Borough. 12 samples were taken, but these all proved on analysis to be genuine lard. Groceries. In all 69 samples of various groceries were taken, and 10 or 14.5 per cent. were reported against. Five of these were tinned goods, two were cases of coffee, one of malt vinegar, and one of cocoa. In the case of the cocoa, which contained 33 per cent. of extraneous shell, the proceedings resulted in the vendor being convicted, and a fine of £1 was inflicted with 12s. 6d. costs. Cereals and Flour. As was stated in my last Annual Report this Council issued a circular letter to all grocers and others within the Borough calling their attention to the so-called "facing of rice," viz.: giving the rice a polished appearance by means of a mineral known as talc or steatite. It is satisfactory to report that the eight samples of rice taken in various parts of the Borough proved on analysis to be entirely free from "facing." In connection with the list of articles sampled under this heading it will be noticed that one rather unusual adulteration was detected, viz.: arrowroot containing 90 per cent. of tapioca flour. A comparison between the price paid for the sample of arrowroot, viz.: 1s. 6d. per pound, and that usually paid for tapioca, viz.: 3d. per pound, shows that a large profit is thereby obtained. 225 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Although adulterations are seldom reported under this heading it shows the necessity of occasional sampling. Meats. It will be seen from Table CIII. that a very high percentage of adulteration was detected under this heading, the adulteration consisting entirely of the addition of a boron preservative, in one case amounting to as much as 55 grains per pound of sausage. In five cases proceedings were taken against the vendors, and convictions obtained. It seems to be the general practice among certain sausage makers to use this boron preservative indiscriminately, and usually by means of a dredger. It is argued from a trade point of view that the addition of a small amount of preservative is absolutely imperative in the manufacture of sausages. Assuming this to be so then the purchaser should be notified of the addition, the amount added should also be stated. Drugs. 19 samples of drugs were taken during the year, and all proved genuine. Tinned Foods. Eight samples were taken and in six of these, or 75 per cent., it was reponed that there was present a small proportion of tin varying from .25 to .95 grains per pound. As the amount was small it was decided by the Public Health Committee not to take proceedings against the vendors. Q 226 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Although there has been a considerable increase in the amount of work, both clerical and otherwise, during the year, I am able to report that it has all been carried out by the staff in a most satisfactory manner. I have the honour to be, Mr. Mayor and Gentlemen, Your obedient Servant, P. CALDWELL SMITH, Medical Officer of Health. APPENDIX.
